The narrow table was stacked with ledgers not my own, with schematics for ventilation systems, with proposed amendments to the municipal code on factory safety. The smell of damp wool and fryer grease had been replaced by the scent of strong coffee, ink, and the faint, metallic trace of type from the proofs Pieter van Houten had begun sending me. The space was still small, still poor, but it had been weaponized. It had intention.

I shrugged off the coat, hanging it on the peg behind the door. From the pocket, I withdrew a single, cream-colored envelope. The paper was thick, expensive. It bore no return address, only my name, written in a sharp, angular hand. I set it on the table, unopened. It could wait. Invitations from the Burgemeester’s newly-formed Industrial Liaison Committee could always wait. They needed the specter of the Visser fire, the living, breathing reminder of what neglect cost. I was their conscience, hired and housed. A useful woman. The irony was a coal I kept warm in my palm.

The knock on my door was not the firm, official rap of the Municipal Guard. It was a quick, syncopated rhythm—one-two, one-two-three. I smiled, a private, genuine thing.

“It’s open, Lena.”

The door swung inward, bringing with it a gust of the canal’s damp air and the vibrant presence of Lena de Wit. She no longer wore the drab, shapeless dress of a Visser floor-girl. Her clothes were still simple—a sturdy skirt, a clean blouse—but they fit. A leather tool-belt was slung low on her hips, a holster for wrenches and calipers. Her hands, scrubbed but permanently etched with the memory of grease, held a paper-wrapped bundle.

“Brought you something,” she said, her voice louder, clearer than it had ever been in the factory’s roar. She tossed the bundle onto the table. It landed with a solid thunk. “From the lads. Well, from Kees, mainly. He’s taken over the smithy on Zoutsteeg, you know. Proper partnership.”

I unwrapped the paper. Inside was a metal flask, hammered steel, simple and flawless. It was cool and heavy in my hand. Engraved on the side, in clean, unadorned letters, was a single word: Vuur. Fire.

“It’s for your coffee,” Lena said, dropping into the room’s other chair without being asked. “Kees said a consultant shouldn’t be drinking from chipped mugs. Gives the wrong impression.”

I ran my thumb over the engraving. A show of teeth, however subtle. “It’s perfect. Tell him thank you.”

“I will.” She leaned back, surveying the room with a foreman’s eye. “Busy.”

“There’s a lot to do. The new regulations are just words on paper. They have to be forced into the world. Like teeth into flesh.”

Lena nodded. She understood force. “We’re starting the inspections at Van Leyden’s mill next week. The owner’s spitting nails, says we’ll bankrupt him with our ‘womanly nerves.’” She grinned, a flash of white in her weathered face. “I told him my nerves are just fine. It’s his drive-belts I’m worried about. They’re frayed enough to make a saint swear.”

This was the new current. Lena worked for the fledgling Worker’s Safety Guild, a body born from the ashes and the public outcry that followed. Pieter’s articles had done their work, painting not a portrait of a tragic accident, but of a calculated neglect. The public, for a moment, had cared. And in that moment, we had built a lever. Lena was the lever’s point, applying pressure where it hurt.

“How’s your mother?” I asked.

Lena’s smile softened, just at the edges. “Better. The cough’s almost gone. The doctor you sent… he actually listened.” She looked at me, the easy bravado fading into something more complex. “She asks after you. Calls you ‘that sharp angel.’ Thinks you’re too thin.”

“I am too thin,” I said, pouring coffee from the pot on the small stove into the new flask. The metal hissed softly. “It’s a professional asset. Makes me look hungry. Which I am.”

We drank in comfortable silence. The camaraderie was a new, strange fabric. It was not friendship, not exactly. It was the mutual recognition of soldiers who had survived the same trench. We had seen the machinery from the inside, felt its teeth. Now we were outside, trying to jam a spar into its gears. It was enough.

After Lena left, the flask of warm coffee in my hand, I went out. The appointment with the journalist was long past; Pieter was more of a colleague now, a co-conspirator in the daylight. This was a different sort of patrol.

I walked east, towards the canals where the dye-works and smaller machine shops clustered. The air here was still thick with the smell of chemicals and hot metal, but the frantic, desperate pace had eased. Or perhaps I was seeing it with new eyes. I paused outside a three-story brick building, its windows newly cleaned, a fresh downspout gleaming against the soot-stained wall.

A man emerged, rolling a handcart loaded with finished brass fittings. He saw me, paused, and touched his cap. Not a gesture of submission, but of recognition.

“Mevrouw Visser.”

“Mijnheer Bakker,” I nodded. This was not the Inspector. This was a different Bakker, a foreman at the very works Hendrik had cited as “unrealistic” for installing a fire-suppression pump. The city had shamed him into it after the articles ran. A small victory.

“Pump’s in and tested,” he said, jerking a thumb back towards the building. “Cost a bleeding fortune. But the men… they sleep better. The wife nags less.” He said it with a grudging respect. The cost was measured in guilders, but the value was in the quieting of a specific, nightly fear. A masculine worry, so often dismissed as feminine nerves, now validated by hardware and law.

“Good,” I said, and meant it.

I walked on. At a corner pub, the kind Hendrik might have frequented, I saw a group of workers at a table. They were not slumped in exhausted silence. They were leaning over a broadsheet, one of Pieter’s, arguing. I caught the words “ventilation” and “mandatory.” Their disagreement was not about if things should change, but how. The seed of the idea had taken root. It was no longer my lonely obsession; it was a collective problem to be solved. The chaos I’d chosen had crystallized into a new, fragile order.

The light was fading, bleeding from grey to indigo, when I found myself at the iron bridge overlooking the North Canal. This was where I’d stood that first night, smelling of smoke and failure, the city a cold, indifferent monster. I leaned on the railing, the hammered flask cool through my gloves.

Below, a barge moved slowly, loaded with timber. A man on deck called out to a woman on the tow-path, his voice carrying clear on the still air. A mundane, human sound. The water reflected the newly-lit gas lamps along the quay, not as a shattered mess, but as long, steady pillars of light.

I thought of the girl in the damp coat, pushing into De Visser’s, all show and no substance. A stray cat. She had been driven by a singular, private hurt: an aunt’s shame, a stolen legacy. She had wanted to claim a thing, a building, a name.

I had claimed nothing. And everything.

I had not gotten my inheritance. I had burned it to the ground. In its place, I had built a reputation. It was a colder, harder currency than brick and mortar. It was a tool. Anika Visser, the fire-woman. The consultant. The sharp angel. A useful woman.

The hook the inspectors had tried to set in me had been swallowed, digested, and the line turned back on the fisherman. The fraud investigation had quietly dissolved, lost in the larger scandal of Hendrik’s negligence. The official report on the fire concluded “faulty wiring,” a polite fiction all parties now upheld. I had met their mechanism with a greater one: the mechanism of public shame, of financial pressure, of cold, hard legal code. It was a man’s game, played with a woman’s weapons—observation, patience, the ruthless exploitation of sentiment.

I was not saved. Salvation implied an end, a resting place. This was a perpetual motion. The soot was gone from my hands, but it was etched under the skin, a permanent blueprint. The world was still made of greed and grinding gears, but I had learned the rhythm of the grind. I could place my words, my very presence, like a grain of sand in the right bearing, and listen for the satisfying shriek of friction.

From my pocket, I finally took the cream-colored envelope. I slit it open with my thumbnail. The note inside was brief.

Mevrouw Visser, Your presence is requested at a private dinner, to discuss the future oversight of the new safety ordinances. The Burgemeester believes a more… integrated approach is warranted. Your unique perspective would be invaluable. Friday, eight o’clock. The House on the Herengracht. S. van der Linde.

Van der Linde. The Burgemeester’s chief fixer. A man who operated in velvet-lined rooms, who turned progress into policy, and policy into patronage. The invitation was not to a battle, but to a negotiation. They wanted to bring the sharp angel inside, to perch her on a gilded shelf. To integrate the chaos into the system, to blunt its teeth.

I looked from the note to the dark water, to the pillars of reflected light. They wanted to give me a seat at the table. A place where the rules were spoken, not broken. Where influence was a subtle poison in a crystal glass.

A smile touched my lips, thin and without warmth. The girl from Chapter 1 would have been cowed by the paper, intimidated by the address. She would have seen it as an end.

I saw it for what it was: a new kind of door. And I knew, with a cold certainty, that I would walk through it. Not to be integrated. But to learn the new rules, so I could break them better.

I folded the note, tucked it back into its expensive envelope. The hook was always there, in different metal, different bait. The current never stopped. You simply learned to swim upstream.

Turning from the canal, I pulled my collar up against the evening chill. The city’s hum deepened with the twilight, a contented, productive purr. I walked into it, the weight of the steel flask a comfortable anchor against my hip. I had an appointment to keep. A table to set. And a new, more dangerous game to learn.

The House on the Herengracht would be warm, no doubt. Dry. Full of polished men with reasoned voices. I wondered what they would smell like, these men who shaped the world from upholstered chairs. I wondered if they would flinch when I walked in, smelling of coffee, cold water, and the faint, indelible scent of smoke.

Chapter 12

Final Chapter

The rain was a soft, persistent whisper against the glass, a gentle argument with the city’s old stones. It beaded on the window of the corner office, distorting the view of the Herengracht’s stately, rain-slicked facades into something liquid and dreamlike. Inside, the air was still, holding the scent of beeswax, old paper, and the faint, expensive ghost of Sandalwood from the man sitting across from me.

Simon van der Linde steepled his fingers, a gesture meant to convey thoughtful authority. His suit was the grey of a dove’s breast, his smile a practiced curve. “You see, the proposal is not about containment. It’s about… channeling. Directing that formidable energy of yours into structures that can sustain it, and amplify it. The Burgemeester envisions a civic oversight role, a liaison between the port authorities and the new community safety initiatives. A voice for the practical realities.”

I let the silence stretch, watching the rain trace paths down the pane. A year ago, in De Visser, silence from a man like this would have been a wall I felt compelled to fill with my own voice, to justify, to explain. Now, I understood it was a tool. Theirs, and mine.

“A voice,” I repeated, my tone flat. “From a seat at a very long table, I imagine. Where the practical realities get translated into sub-committee reports.”

Van der Linde’s smile didn’t falter, but it cooled a degree. “Progress requires procedure, my dear. The alternative is anarchy. Or,” he paused, letting the alternative hang, “a continued existence on the periphery, where one’s victories are necessarily… messy. And temporary.”

My dear. The needle, now sheathed in velvet. I thought of the fryer-grease smell of my old coat, the guttural murmur of De Visser falling silent as I entered. The periphery. That’s where they’d always wanted me, where women like me belonged: outside, looking in, or inside only on suffrance, a curiosity. The choice he offered was a classic one: step inside, be legitimized, be neutered. Or stay outside, be powerful in bursts, be alone.

I rose from the leather chair and walked to the window. The canal below was a black mirror, holding the golden coins of streetlamps. A barge slid past, silent. “You’re right,” I said, my back to him. “The fire at the Westindische warehouse was messy. Exposing the smuggling ring in the customs house was messy. Stopping the Kinderdijk land-grab was exceptionally messy. For your friends.”

I turned. He was watching me, the polished mask still in place, but his eyes were calculating, re-assessing the equation in the room. I was no longer the sharp, ragged creature pulled from the water, grateful for a towel. I was the woman who had drained that water, poisoned the well for his associates, and now stood, dry and deliberate, in his sanctum.

“I’m not interested in a voice at your table, Meneer van der Linde,” I said, my voice calm in the hushed room. “I’m interested in the ledger.”

A flicker. A tiny crack. “The ledger?”

“The one that doesn’t appear in the sub-committee reports. The one that shows who really owns the derelict properties slated for ‘urban renewal,’ who profits from the safety inspections they delay, which cousins get the tender for the new dike works.” I took a step toward the desk. “The practical reality is that your system runs on two engines: the public one you talk about, and the private one you don’t. I don’t want to lubricate the public one. I want the schematics for the private one.”

He laughed then, a dry, papery sound. “You are proposing blackmail.”

“I am proposing a new understanding,” I corrected. “I don’t take a seat. I become a… consultant. Unlisted. My work continues. But now, instead of breaking your windows to get your attention, I use the back door. And in return, certain windows remain unbroken. Certain ledgers remain private.”

The gender of it was not lost on either of us. He offered the public, masculine sphere: a title, a procedure, a visible but impotent place. I demanded the private, the hidden, the realm of influence and whispered deals—traditionally also a masculine preserve, but one accessed through manipulation, through the informal power they always assumed women wielded in shadows. I was claiming it outright, as a seat of power, not a back alley.

He leaned back, the chair creaking softly. “And if we refuse this… understanding?”

I allowed my own smile, the thin, warmthless one I’d felt on the canal side weeks ago. I reached into the inner pocket of my jacket—not the old wool coat, but a tailored piece of dark tweed—and pulled out a single sheet of paper. I placed it on the immaculate blotter before him. It was a copy of a shipping manifest, a list of container numbers. At the bottom, in my handwriting, was a single name: the Burgemeester’s brother-in-law.

“Then the next mess will require more than a procedural review to clean up,” I said softly. “It will require a new election.”

He looked at the paper, then up at me. The calculation in his eyes was gone, replaced by a cold, clear recognition. He saw not a woman to be managed, but a force to be reckoned with. A counterpart.

“Friday, eight o’clock,” he said finally, his voice devoid of its earlier paternal cadence. It was flat, professional. “There is a dining club. De Zilveren Egel. We will discuss the parameters of your consultancy.”

I nodded. “I’ll bring my own pen.”

I didn’t wait for a dismissal. I turned and walked out of the office, past the silent, curious secretary, and into the carpeted hall. The warmth of the building was climate-controlled, scentless, a different kind of physical blow than De Visser’s had been. It was the warmth of preserved power, of decisions made far from the rain.

The rain had settled into a drizzle as I walked. I didn’t head for the tram or call a cab. I walked east, leaving the symmetrical grace of the Herengracht for the tighter, darker arteries of the old port district. The smells changed gradually: less wax and perfume, more wet stone, diesel, and the ripe, living smell of the river.

I found myself outside De Visser. The neon sign buzzed, flickering slightly, painting the wet cobbles a sickly orange. I pushed the door open.

The warmth inside was a familiar embrace, thick with the smell of stale beer, brine, and the honest sweat of a day’s labor. The conversation—a low, guttural murmur—did not lull. A few heads turned, but the glances were different now. There was no curiosity, no challenge. A man at the bar—Pieter, who worked the winches on the Komet—gave a slow, almost imperceptible nod. Another, at a corner table, raised his glass a half-inch before returning to his conversation.

I was no longer a woman out of place. I was a feature of the landscape. The woman who had broken the Westindische syndicate. The one who’d made sure the widow Van Holt got her pension after her husband was killed in a “flagged” accident. I had earned my silence here.

Marta was behind the bar, polishing a glass with a cloth. She saw me and her face, usually a monument of weary stoicism, softened at the edges. “The usual?”

“Please.”

She poured a jenever, the glass clinking softly against the bottle’s neck. She set it before me, then leaned her elbows on the bar, lowering her voice. “Heard you had a meeting with the velvet glove.”

News traveled fast in the right channels. “I did.”

“And?”

“We reached an understanding. He keeps his velvet clean. I keep my knives sharp.”

Marta snorted, a short, approving sound. “Good. Men like that, they think a chair is a gift. They don’t know it’s just another thing to tie you down.” She looked me over. “You look different. Not just the clothes.”

I knew what she meant. The anxiety that used to live in my shoulders, the constant hum of what if I’m wrong, what if they see through me, was gone. It had been replaced by a low, steady current of certainty. I had been wrong. I had been seen through. And I had survived it. I had used it. The fear hadn’t vanished; it had been metabolized into fuel.

“I’m not waiting for the hook anymore, Marta,” I said, sipping the sharp, clean liquor. “I’m reading the current.”

She gave that nod, the one that was the highest form of praise in this room. “About time.”

I finished my drink, the heat of it spreading through my chest. I left coins on the bar, another ritual now understood. As I turned to go, the door opened, letting in a gust of damp air and a young woman. She was maybe twenty, her coat too thin for the weather, her eyes scanning the room with a mixture of defiance and trepidation. The conversation dipped, just for a second. Heads turned.

She saw the looks, hunched her shoulders, and made for a small, empty table near the back, trying to project an indifference she clearly didn’t feel.

I stopped. I felt every eye in the room, felt the weight of the moment. The old script demanded I leave, that I mind my own business, that I prove I belonged by not rocking this particular boat. The new script, the one I was writing, demanded something else.

I walked over to her table. She looked up, wary, ready for a challenge.

“The jenever is good here,” I said, my voice neutral. “But the bitterballen are better if you’re hungry. Marta behind the bar knows everyone. If you’re looking for work, tell her what you can do. If you’re in trouble, tell her what you need. She doesn’t suffer fools, but she doesn’t judge the desperate either.”

The girl stared at me, her defiance melting into confusion, then a dawning, cautious understanding. She gave a tiny nod.

I didn’t smile. I just returned the nod, then turned and walked out, leaving the warmth of the bodies and the murmur of their talk behind me.

The apartment was quiet. It was no longer just a place to hide; it was a place to think. The files were gone from the floor, sorted, actioned, or archived. A single, sturdy desk stood by the window, holding a modern telephone, a ledger of my own, and a framed photograph: me and the team from the Komet, taken the day after the customs house sting, all of us grinning under a pale spring sun.

I shrugged off my tweed jacket and hung it up. From a small cabinet, I took out the steel flask. It was dented, its surface scratched from a year in my pocket, through fights and flights and long, cold watches. I unscrewed the cap and poured a measure of the same jenever into a small glass. I didn’t drink to forget or to fortify myself. I drank to mark the moment. To taste the difference.

I sat at the desk and opened my ledger. Not Van der Linde’s hidden one, but mine. A record of balances. Debts settled. Promises kept. Loose ends. There were fewer now. The thread of the Westindische fire was tied off, its arsonist awaiting trial, his backers nervously silent. The Kinderdijk land scheme was unspooled, the documents now in the hands of a newspaper editor who owed me nothing but a good story. The man who had tried to have me drowned in the cold canal water was serving time for tax evasion, a fittingly mundane end.

The major threads were resolved. But a story isn’t a piece of knitting; it doesn’t end with a neat, clipped yarn. It frays into the future. My thread was now woven into the fabric of the city—into the wary respect of the port, into the cold calculus of the Herengracht, into the uncertain hope in a young woman’s eyes in a bar. I was part of its ecosystem now, a predator, a cleaner, a fixed point.

The lens through which I’d seen everything—gender—hadn’t shattered. It had clarified. It was no longer just a barrier; it was a tool, a disguise, a weapon, a source of strength they consistently underestimated. I hadn’t overcome it. I had integrated it. I was not a woman acting like a man in a man’s world. I was a woman operating in a world I was helping to reshape, using every tool my experience had given me.

I closed the ledger. The invitation to De Zilveren Egel lay beside it. A new door. A more dangerous game, played on polished floors with unspoken rules. I felt no anxiety, only a focused curiosity. I would go. I would listen. I would learn the new language of power, the one spoken in nods and silences and the strategic placement of names. And I would use it, not to join their club, but to ensure my own territory remained inviolate. To ensure that the Martas of this city had a little more leverage, that the young women walking into hostile rooms felt a little less alone.

The rain had stopped. Outside, the city gleamed under a sky washed clean, the lights of the port and the stadhuis reflecting in the countless puddles like scattered constellations. The hum was there, the deep, contented purr, but I could hear the individual notes within it now: the groan of a ship, the distant clang of a tram, the laughter from an open café door.

I finished the jenever, the final warmth of it spreading through me. I placed the glass down, the click firm and final in the quiet room.

I had arrived here a year ago, a needle driven by the rain, seeking only shelter from the storm. I was leaving this chapter not as a needle, but as a compass. The storm wasn’t over; the storm was the world. But I had built a boat. I had charted the currents. I had learned to sail.

I turned off the desk lamp, leaving the room in the soft, blue-grey light of the city at night. I didn’t look back at the room. I looked out the window, at the endless, shifting pattern of light and shadow and water, at the great, sleeping beast of the city.

I pulled on my jacket, my fingers brushing the cool metal of the flask in its pocket. A familiar anchor. A reminder of where the waterline was.

I opened the door to the hall and stepped out, not into an ending, but into the perpetual, resonant beginning of the next thing. The corridor stretched before me, leading to the stairs, to the street, to the next door waiting to be pushed open.

I walked toward it, ready.

Chapter 13

Chapter 13

The invitation from Egel arrived not by post, but by courier. A thick, cream-colored card, the address embossed in a severe, elegant type. It was placed directly into my hand by a young man in a dark coat who waited on the landing until I took it, his expression as blank as a closed door. No words were exchanged. The transaction was complete the moment the paper left his gloved fingers and touched mine.

I carried it inside, to the desk by the window. The city below was a study in muted golds and deep blues, the evening light lying across the rooftops like worn velvet. I did not open it immediately. I let it sit on the scarred wood, a foreign artifact in my familiar space. Its presence was a pressure, a slight but definite shift in the atmosphere of the room, like the arrival of a silent, well-dressed guest.

Finally, I slid a thumb under the flap. The paper gave way with a soft, expensive sigh.

Mevrouw de Vries, it began. Not ‘Els’. The formality was the first move. You are requested for an evening of conversation at the residence of Mijnheer A. Egel, Keizersgracht 724. Eight o’clock. Informal.

The date was tomorrow. The assumption of availability, the second move. ‘Informal’ was the third, and the most complex. In the world of the jenever glass and the zinc counter, informal meant rolled sleeves and loosened collars. Here, it was a code. It meant no tails, no ballgowns, but it did not mean no armor. It meant the knives would be smaller, better concealed, their cuts delivered with a smile.

I placed the card back on the desk. A focused curiosity, that was what I had felt. It remained, but it was now cold, a surgeon’s curiosity. I would go. I would listen. I would learn the language. But first, I had to choose my own uniform for this informal war.

The dress was a weapon, and like any good weapon, it was a compromise. I found it in a small, stubborn shop run by a widow whose eyes held the same flinty assessment as my own. It was a deep, smoky grey, the color of the North Sea under a winter sky. The cut was severe, almost masculine in the line of the shoulders, but the fabric was a heavy, fluid silk that moved with a whisper. It covered me from throat to wrist to ankle, yet the way it fell suggested the shape beneath without concession. It was armor, but armor that acknowledged the battlefield. It said: I see your game. I am playing. But I have not forgotten my own rules.

The widow, Marta—of course her name was Marta—nodded once as I turned before her mirror. “It needs no jewelry,” she said, her voice like rustling paper. “It would be an insult. Like gilding a rifle.”

I paid her in cash, the notes passing between us with the solemnity of a treaty. She wrapped the dress in tissue, then in plain brown paper. “Good luck,” she said, as I took the parcel. It wasn’t a pleasantry. It was an intelligence report from a veteran of a different, older front.

The night of the invitation, I dressed slowly. The silk was cool against my skin. I did my hair, pinning it back in a way that was neat but not ornate. My face in the mirror was pale, sharp, all angles and watchfulness. I looked like what I was: a creature from a different ecosystem, carefully adapted for a night in a more rarefied atmosphere. I would not blend. I would contrast.

The final touch was the flask. I filled it with the good jenever, the one Jan kept under the counter for special occasions, which usually meant funerals or near-death experiences. I slid it into a small, matching grey silk pouch I’d stitched, and tucked it into the hidden pocket within the seam of the dress. An anchor. A reminder of the waterline.

A taxi, summoned by a call from the café phone, took me to the Keizersgracht. The canal houses floated in the darkness, their gabled facades illuminated like stage sets, golden light spilling from enormous windows onto the black water. Number 724 was a giant, a monument to restrained wealth. I paid the driver and stood on the cobbles, looking up. The hum of the city was different here. It was the hum of money, of engines purring inside polished automobiles, of crystal clinking, a higher, more brittle frequency.

The door was opened by a man in a black suit before I could lift the knocker. He was older, his face a mask of professional neutrality. “Mevrouw de Vries,” he said, stepping back. He knew. They all knew. I was expected, my name and likely my biography already circulated among the staff. I was the evening’s novelty.

I crossed the threshold into a silence so profound it felt like a physical substance. The entrance hall was a vault of marble and dark wood, a single, monstrous flower arrangement on a central table screaming color into the stillness. My footsteps were swallowed by an immense Persian carpet. The air smelled of beeswax, old books, and the faint, dry scent of orchids.

“The gathering is in the library,” the man said, gesturing to an arched doorway. “May I take your coat?”

I had no coat. I had only the dress and the hidden flask. “No, thank you,” I said. My voice, used to cutting through the din of De Visser, sounded too loud, too raw in the cushioned quiet. I saw the faintest flicker in his eyes. Another note for his report: Declines courtesy. Possibly armed.

I walked toward the arch, the silk whispering a warning with every step.

The library was not a room; it was a landscape. Two stories of books climbed the walls, accessed by a slender, wrought-iron gallery. A fire crackled in a fireplace large enough to roast an ox, and the light from it danced across the faces of a dozen people. They stood in small constellations, holding glasses of wine or whiskey. The conversation was a low, polished murmur.

Every head turned as I entered. The murmur did not stop, but it dipped, recalibrated. I was a new variable in the equation.

Egel detached himself from a group near the fire. He wore a dark suit, no tie, the ‘informality’ on him looking like a statesman’s leisure. “Els,” he said, extending a hand. He used my first name here, in his fortress. A reclamation of territory. “I’m so pleased you could come.”

I took his hand. His grip was firm, dry, assessing. “Mijnheer Egel. Thank you for the invitation.”

“Aad, please. We are informal.” His smile was a professional tool, well-maintained. “Come, let me introduce you.”

The introductions were a masterclass in the new language. He did not say, “This is Els de Vries, she runs a questionable café and solves problems for desperate women.” He said, “This is Mevrouw de Vries. She has an remarkable understanding of our city’s less-charted neighborhoods.” Or, “Mevrouw de Vries possesses a singular practical expertise.”

The men shook my hand, their eyes performing a rapid, professional appraisal—not of my body, but of my potential utility or threat. Their handshakes were brief, their nods precise. The women were different. Their smiles were perfect, their greetings warm, but their eyes held a deeper, more complex calculus. They were experts in this environment, fluent in its subtle hierarchies. They saw the dress, understood its statement, and were determining whether I was a challenger, a curiosity, or a potential ally in their own, unspoken games. One, a woman in her fifties with a face of serene, sharp intelligence and a necklace of raw amber, held my gaze a beat longer. “I’ve heard your name,” she said, her voice soft. “In interesting contexts.”

“I find interesting contexts have a way of finding me, Mevrouw…?”

“Van Dijk.” She smiled, and it reached her eyes. “The feeling is mutual.”

Egel guided me to a sideboard where a man in a white jacket stood behind an array of bottles. “What may I offer you? We have an excellent Bordeaux, or a single malt, if you prefer.”

The jenever in my hidden flask called to me, a base note of the real world. But to drink it here would be to raise a flag of otherness, a declaration of separation. To accept their wine was to play their game on their board.

“Whiskey,” I said. “Neat.”

A slight elevation of Egel’s eyebrow. A small point scored, or perhaps ceded. The barman poured two fingers of a tawny gold liquid into a heavy crystal glass. I took it, let the heat of the spirit bloom in the space between my palm and the cool crystal.

For an hour, I sailed. I moved from constellation to constellation, a dark star passing through their ordered heavens. I listened. The talk was of politics, but not the street-level graft I knew; it was of policy, of influence, of the gentle steering of great ships of state and commerce. It was of art acquisitions, of charitable foundations, of zoning laws for the docklands redevelopment. Names were dropped like chess pieces: the Burgemeester, a certain Minister, the head of the Port Authority. I said little. I asked concise questions. I learned that a man’s true opinion was often revealed in what he dismissed, and a woman’s power was often located in the quiet amendment she made to her husband’s statement.

I was circling back toward the fire, a refill of whiskey in my hand—a prop, mostly, its level barely lowered—when I saw him. He stood slightly apart, near the French doors leading to a terrace, studying a globe in a stand. He was younger than most here, perhaps in his late thirties, with a lean, restless frame that seemed uncomfortable in its well-tailored clothes. His hair was a shade too long, his posture lacking the ingrained, relaxed authority of the other men. He turned as I approached, and his eyes were not assessing. They were interested, openly and without guile.

“Contemplating escape?” I asked, nodding toward the terrace and the dark garden beyond.

He smiled, a quick, genuine thing. “Is it that obvious? I find these things… a bit airless.” He gestured with his own glass toward the room. “I’m Simon.”

“Els.”

“I know.” He leaned a shoulder against the bookshelf. “You’re the topic of the week. The mysterious woman from the docks who helped old Martens’ daughter. And who told Aad Egel ‘no’, then ‘maybe’, all in one conversation.”

So the story had traveled, polished and simplified in the telling. “The facts are roughly correct. The mystery is overstated.”

“I doubt that.” He took a drink. “Aad’s fascinated. He collects unique things. People included.”

“And you? What do you collect?”

“Problems,” he said, and the smile turned wry. “I’m a lawyer. A bad one, according to my father.” He nodded toward a tall, silver-haired man holding court by the mantel. “He thinks I should be managing the family holdings, not defending squatters’ rights or fighting the Port Authority on behalf of small fishermen.”

The statement was a grenade rolled casually onto the polished floor. It was a test. I watched it lie there between us. “That sounds like more interesting work,” I said, neutrally.

His eyes lit. “It is. It’s also a fantastic way to become the disappointing son.” He looked at me directly. “You’ve made a space for yourself outside all this. That’s… impressive.”

“It’s necessary,” I corrected. “Spaces don’t exist. They’re taken.”

Before he could reply, Egel was beside us, his timing impeccable. “Simon, monopolizing our guest of honor?” His tone was avuncular, but there was a steel thread in it. “Els, there’s someone I’d like you to meet. A fellow pragmatist.”

Simon gave a slight, almost imperceptible shrug of surrender. “A pleasure, Mevrouw de Vries.”

Egel led me away, his hand a light, proprietary pressure on my elbow. He steered me to a corner where a man stood alone, watching the room with the calm, detached gaze of a sniper. He was compact, bald, his face unreadable.

“Els, this is Maarten Brink. Maarten handles… special logistics for several of our civic projects.”

Brink offered a hand. His grip was crushing, brief, and devoid of any human warmth. It was a functional grip, like operating a lever. “De Vries.” His voice was a low rasp. “Egel speaks highly of your resourcefulness.”

“I solve local problems,” I said.

“So do I.” His eyes were the color of old ice. “On a larger scale. The docklands redevelopment, for instance. A complex problem. Many moving parts. Some of them… resistant.”

The conversation with Simon about fishermen and squatters’ rights echoed in my head. I took a sip of whiskey, let the burn focus me. “Resistance is often a matter of perspective.”

“Perspective can be adjusted,” Brink said evenly. “With the right leverage. Or the right removal of leverage.” He paused, his gaze fixed on me. “I understand you have a talent for finding leverage in unexpected places. That is a valuable talent. Aligning local… stability… with broader progress is crucial.”

It was not a threat. It was colder than that. It was a strategic assessment. He was measuring me, not as a woman or a curiosity, but as a piece on the board. A piece that could be useful, or could become an obstruction to be cleared.

Egel watched us, a benign smile on his face. He had brought me here for this. Not for the wine or the firelight, but for this moment in the corner. To see how I would stand under Brink’s glacial gaze. To see if I understood the game being played on the polished floors.

“Stability is my primary concern,” I said, meeting Brink’s eyes. “The stability of my neighborhood. My people. Progress that shatters that is just a different kind of storm.”

Brink’s lips twitched in something that was not a smile. “Storms pass. The landscape after is what matters.”

The verbal chess match was broken by Van Dijk, the woman with the amber necklace. She slipped her arm through mine with a practiced ease. “Aad, Maarten, you are being dreadfully serious. You’ll frighten her away. Come, Els, I want your opinion on a terrible painting my husband insists on buying.”

She drew me away, her grip firm. When we were out of earshot, near a hideous, gilded landscape, she dropped the social smile. Her voice was low and urgent. “Be careful of Brink. He is not a man. He is a function. A demolition function.”

“Why tell me this?”

“Because you are a new piece on the board,” she said, echoing my own thought. “And the board is tipping. My husband is on the planning committee. Brink’s ‘special logistics’ for the docklands involve clearing the old warehouses—the ones full of artists, and squatters, and those fishermen your new friend Simon is so fond of. Quietly. Efficiently. No messy protests.”

“And Egel?”

“Aad believes in order. A clean, prosperous city. He sees Brink as the broom that sweeps clean. He sees you as someone who understands the dust.” She patted my arm, the social smile returning as a man approached. “Think on it, my dear. The invitation tonight was not to socialize. It was to observe the tools in the shed.”

The evening wound down. Goodbyes were performed, a symphony of insincere warmth and promised future meetings. Egel saw me to the door himself. “I hope it was not too tedious,” he said.

“It was illuminating,” I replied.

“Good. We should talk again soon. About the city. Our shared interests.” He held the door open. The cold night air rushed in, a shocking, welcome relief. “A car is waiting for you.”

I did not argue. The black sedan idled at the curb. I slid into the back seat, the scent of leather and privilege filling my lungs. As the car pulled away from the glowing monument of 724 Keizersgracht, I finally let my breath out, a long, slow stream in the dark.

I had learned the language. I had seen the board. Brink was the threat, a cold force of eradication disguised as progress. Egel was the architect, willing to use any tool. Simon was a potential fracture line, a source of information. Van Dijk was an ally of convenience, a player with her own agenda.

And I was not a piece to be moved. I was a fact. A piece of the landscape they intended to remodel.

The car dropped me at the foot of my own street. I walked the last distance, the cobbles hard and familiar under my thin soles. The hum here was my hum: the distant shout, the bass from a basement bar, the smell of fried dough and wet stone.

Inside my room, I shut the door on the world of crystal and quiet threats. I stripped off the grey silk, hung it carefully. It had served its purpose. In my underclothes, I pulled the flask from its hidden pocket, unscrewed the cap, and took a long, unmediated swallow of jenever. The fire of it was truth, was home.

I stood at the window, looking at my city, the real one. The great, sleeping beast. Brink and Egel saw it as a machine to be optimized, a canvas to be whitewashed. They saw the warehouses as blots, the fishermen as rust, the Martas as sentimental clutter.

I saw the beast’s ecosystem. The warehouses were its hollow bones, sheltering life in the marrow. The fishermen were its link to the deep, old blood of the sea. The Martas were its immune system.

They planned a demolition. A clean, efficient removal of leverage.

I felt no anxiety. Only a cold, focused clarity. They had shown me their hand. They believed their game, played on polished floors with unspoken rules, was the only one that mattered.

But I knew a secret. The most dangerous game is not played in a library by a fire. It is played in the streets, in the rain, in the spaces between. It is played by those with nothing to lose but their place in the ecosystem. I had built a boat. I had charted the currents. And I knew how to sail into a storm, or how to create one.

I would not fight their war on their terms. I would not plead or protest.

I would go to the warehouses. I would find the artists, the squatters, the fishermen Simon tried to defend. I would not offer help. I would offer a simple, brutal equation: their problem was my problem. Brink’s “special logistics” relied on fear, on isolation, on picking off the weak one by one.

I would make that impossible. I would weave the disparate, resistant threads of the docklands into a net. A net strong enough to snag the broom that sought to sweep them clean.

I finished the jenever, the final warmth a covenant with myself. The invitation had been a door into their world. My response would be a door of my own making, slammed shut in theirs, protecting what was mine.

The city gleamed below, its constellations of light reflected in a thousand dark puddles. The storm wasn’t coming. I was the storm. And I would break where I chose.

Chapter 14

Chapter 14

The rain had gentled to a persistent, whispering drizzle by the time I reached the docklands. It was the kind of rain that didn’t fall so much as it seeped, finding every weakness in the asphalt, every crack in the mortar, the very air a cold, damp cloth pressed against the face. The city’s polished heart was miles away, a memory of electric light and silent carpets. Here, the only light was the sickly orange glow of the few streetlamps still functioning, their light swallowed by the fog rolling in off the water. The smell was different, too—not fryer grease and stale beer, but wet rust, rotting wood, and the profound, ancient stink of the river at low tide.

The warehouses loomed like the skeletons of beached whales, their vast, dark shapes hunched against the skyline. Brink and Egel saw blots. I saw topography. I saw the high ground.

My first stop was the one they called the Bakstenen Reus—the Brick Giant. It was the largest of the condemned structures, a fortress of crumbling red brick and boarded-up windows. Inside, it was a kingdom of echoes and stolen electricity. The ground floor was a cavernous space, the ceiling lost in shadow, strung with a web of extension cords that fed a constellation of bare bulbs. They illuminated a chaotic, vibrant sprawl: makeshift studios partitioned by stained bedsheets, sculptures welded from scrap metal looming like silent sentinels, the acrid tang of oil paint and turpentine cutting through the damp.

Heads lifted as I entered, a dozen pairs of eyes catching the movement from their canvases and workbenches. The conversation, a low murmur of ideas and complaint, didn’t stop, but it changed pitch. It became a watchful hum. I was an anomaly here, too. Not a stray cat this time, but a bird of prey landing in a dovecote. My coat was too good, my posture too straight. I carried the chill of the outside world in with me.

A man peeled away from a group huddled around a space heater, wiping his hands on paint-stained trousers. He was young, with a sharp, intelligent face and eyes that were older than the rest of him. Raf, I remembered. Simon had pointed him out once, called him the unofficial mayor of the Reus.

“Els,” he said, not a question. His voice was flat, devoid of Jan’s transactional gruffness. This was a different currency. “Heard you were asking around. After what happened to Simon.”

I didn’t nod. I held his gaze, let the silence between us stretch until it became its own kind of answer. “I’m not here to ask,” I said. My voice was quiet, but it carried in the echo chamber of the space. A few more people looked over. “I’m here to tell. The eviction notices on these walls? They’re not the city’s. They’re a private concern. A company called Brink Holdings.”

Raf’s expression didn’t flicker. “We know who owns the paper. It doesn’t change the locks they’ll put on the doors.”

“It does,” I said. I took a step further in, letting the weak light fall on my face. “Because the city follows rules. Slow, stupid, bureaucratic rules. Appeals. Hearings. Delays. Brink doesn’t. He uses ‘special logistics.’ Men who don’t wear uniforms, who work at night. Who make problems disappear before the sun comes up.”

I saw it then, the minute tightening around his eyes. They’d seen it already. A slashed canvas. Tools gone missing. A fire in a trash bin that got a little too close to the bedding area. Nuisance stuff. The kind that breeds a slow, grinding fear.

“What’s your interest?” a woman’s voice cut in. She emerged from behind a large, abstract canvas streaked with furious reds and blacks. Marta. Of course there was a Marta here. Her hair was tied back in a practical knot, her hands raw and chapped. An artist, but her eyes were the same as all the Martas—assessing, pragmatic, rooted in the physical world.

“They made it my interest,” I said, turning to include her. “They tried to buy my silence. My compliance. They showed me their plans. A clean sweep. Art galleries where this stands. Wine bars where the fishermen mend their nets. They don’t see you. They see rust. Sentimental clutter.”

The word clutter did what my presence could not. It sparked a low flame in the watchful eyes around me. Insult was a language they understood far better than threat.

“So you come to warn us?” Raf asked, a thread of skepticism woven through his tone. “A civic duty?”

I let out a short, breath that wasn’t a laugh. “I don’t do duty. I do ecosystems. They’re picking you off one by one because you’re alone. The artists here. The squatters in the Noordelijke Loods. The old men on the fishing quay. Separate threads. Easy to snap.”

Marta crossed her arms. “And you’re going to weave us together?”

“No,” I said. The simplicity of the word hung in the air. “You’re going to weave yourselves. I’m just giving you the pattern. Their strength is secrecy, speed, fear. Your weakness is isolation. Remove the isolation, and their strength becomes a liability. A man swinging a broom in an empty room is efficient. A man swinging a broom in a room full of tripwires and glue is a fool.”

I reached into my coat, not for a weapon, but for a folded sheaf of papers. Basic schematics of the dockland blocks, drawn from memory and city archives. I laid them on a nearby worktable stained with a rainbow of pigments. “You have lookouts. Use them. Not just for your own door. The quay is your door. The Loods is your door. One sees something, a van, men with tools after dark, they don’t hide. They sound an alarm. A specific one. A light pattern. A siren from that old boat horn on the quay. You all hear it, you all respond. Not to fight. To witness. To be a crowd. To take pictures with those phones you charge on stolen current. Brink’s men are ghosts. Flood them with light.”

Raf was studying the map, his finger tracing the lines between warehouses. “A neighborhood watch. For squatters.”

“A net,” I corrected. “A single, irritable organism. You make it too loud, too messy, too visible for their special logistics. They rely on you being ashamed, being illegal, being afraid of the proper authorities. Stop being afraid of the authorities. Start making the authorities afraid of the scene you’ll create if they let Brink’s cleaners operate.”

Marta was watching me, her head tilted. “This is a war,” she said, not accusingly, but as a statement of fact.

“It’s pest control,” I replied, meeting her eyes. “And you are not the pests.”

There was a longer silence. It was no longer wary, but calculating. I could see the minds working, connecting the dots between their scattered domains. The idea was not charity. It was not solidarity in the bleeding-heart sense. It was tactical, selfish, and brutal. It appealed to them because it was real.

“And you?” Raf asked finally. “Where are you in this net?”

I smiled then, a thin, cold show of teeth. “I’m the one who knows where the broom is stored. And I’m the one who will break its handle.”

I left them with the map, with the idea, a seed planted in fertile, resentful ground. My next stop was the fishing quay, a narrower, smellier kingdom of its own. The men here were older, their resistance not of paint and theory, but of habit and salt-cured stubbornness. They stood in small groups under tarpaulin awnings, mending nets with swift, gnarled fingers, their conversations a series of grunts and observations about the wind.

Their leader was not a leader at all, but the oldest of them, a man named Piet with a face like a weathered cliff and eyes the color of the winter sea. He listened to my same, stripped-down explanation, his hands never stopping their rhythmic work with the net needle.

When I finished, he spat a stream of tobacco juice into the dark water lapping at the pilings. “Crowds. Lights. Nonsense. When they come, a crowbar is a better argument.”

“A crowbar gets you arrested,” I said, leaning against a moss-slick bollard. “A crowd of fifty people with cameras gets you on the evening news. Which one costs Brink more?”

Piet’s needle paused for a fraction of a second. His calculus was different. It wasn’t about art or principle. It was about cost. Annoyance. “My grandson,” he said, nodding toward a sullen young man scaling fish with violent efficiency. “He has one of those phones. Always filming nonsense.”

“Good,” I said. “Let him film. The world loves an old fisherman getting pushed around by men in black jackets. It’s a better story than a new wine bar.”

A grim, toothless approximation of a smile touched Piet’s mouth. “Aye. We’re picturesque, until we’re in the way.” He looked out at the river, at the fog swallowing the opposite bank. “The alarm. The boat horn. We can do that. For Simon.”

It wasn’t agreement out of strategy. It was a debt, paid in vigilance. It was stronger.

By the time I approached the Noordelijke Loods, the night had deepened. This warehouse was the most derelict, home not to artists but to those who had simply run out of everywhere else to go. The air smelled of damp concrete, unwashed bodies, and the sweet-sour tang of desperation. Here, the fear was a living thing, a cold draft that coiled around the ankles.

I was met not by a spokesperson, but by a wall of silent, hostile stares from the shadows. A woman eventually stepped forward, wrapped in a blanket, her hair a wild tangle. She didn’t ask my name. “You bring trouble or food?”

“Information,” I said, keeping my hands visible. “The trouble is already coming. The information might blunt it.”

I gave the same speech, stripped even barer. No talk of ecosystems or art. Just cause and effect. Men will come to burn you out. Together with the others, you can make it too difficult. You can make them choose a softer target.

“Why would the artists care about us?” someone muttered from the darkness.

“They wouldn’t,” I said, my voice cutting through the gloom. “And you wouldn’t care about them. But you both care about not being thrown into the rain. That’s the only thread you need. Hold onto it.”

The woman in the blanket studied me. Her eyes were exhausted, but not stupid. “You’re the one from the bar. The one who knew Simon.”

“Yes.”

She nodded, once. “We listen for the horn.”

It was done. The connections were made, not with friendship, but with the brittle, functional glue of shared threat. I had turned the docklands from a collection of targets into a single, prickly hide. Walking back through the drizzle, I felt the cold, focused clarity solidify into something harder. A plan. A weapon I had forged from the very resentment Brink had sought to exploit.

I didn’t go home. I went to De Visser. The warmth was a familiar slap, the murmur a familiar soundtrack. Jan saw my face and poured the jenever without a word. I took the glass, the first real heat of the day spreading from my gut to my frozen fingers.

I had set the stage. Now I needed the players. Brink’s “special logistics” would be a team, likely subcontracted, men who traded violence for cash and discretion. They would have a base, a van, a routine. I needed to find it. To see their faces. To know the broom before it started to swing.

The door to the bar opened, letting in a gust of wet, cold air. A man entered, shaking water from his dark wool coat. He wasn’t a regular. His shoes were too clean, his frame too broad beneath the tailored fabric. He didn’t look around with the curiosity of a stranger, but with the scanning assessment of someone checking a perimeter.

He walked to the bar, two stools down from me, and ordered a beer. His voice was low, accentless. He paid with exact change. He didn’t drink, just turned the bottle slowly on the zinc, watching the condensation make a wet ring.

He was a professional. And he was here, in this bar at the edge of the docklands, on this particular night.

My pulse, which had been a slow, cold metronome, didn’t quicken. It deepened. Became a heavier, more deliberate thud. I took a slow sip of my jenever, letting the heat bloom on my tongue.

They weren’t waiting for the eviction date. They were doing reconnaissance. Softening the ground. Perhaps they’d heard a whisper that the disparate threads were stirring.

The man’s eyes, reflected in the smudged mirror behind the bar, slid over the room and, for a fraction of a second, met mine. There was no recognition in them. Only a flat, empty evaluation. A cataloging of assets and obstacles. To him, I was just a woman in a bar, of no more consequence than the stool she sat on.

He finished his beer, left the bottle precisely centered on its ring of moisture, and walked back out into the rain.

I counted to thirty. Then I laid coins on the bar, a silent transaction Jan acknowledged with a blink.

Outside, the drizzle had turned back into a needling rain. The street was empty, but the taillights of a dark sedan were just disappearing around the distant corner, heading not toward the city’s bright center, but deeper into the industrial gloom, toward the river’s most forgotten bend.

A location. A direction.

The net was strung. The broom was on the move.

And I knew where it was going to sleep tonight.

Chapter 15

Chapter 15

The river’s forgotten bend was a graveyard of ambition. Hulks of old cranes stood against the bruised sky like the skeletons of giant, arthritic birds. Warehouses, their windows boarded or gaping, slumped against one another for support. The rain here was different. It didn’t fall from the sky so much as it seeped from the bricks, dripped from rusted iron, pooled in the cracked concrete underfoot—a cold, persistent exhalation of the place itself.

I kept to the shadows where they were deep, moved through the patches of sickly yellow light from the few working streetlamps where they were not. My coat was a sodden weight. The jenever’s warmth was a memory in my veins, replaced by a sharp, clean clarity. The dark sedan was parked, empty, outside a two-story brick building that might have once been a customs office. A faint, rhythmic glow came from a second-floor window—the sweep of a flashlight beam.

They were inside. Doing their inventory.

I circled the block, my boots making no sound in the oily grit. The building’s back was a cliff-face of brick, scarred by the ghost-lines of torn-down fire escapes. A single metal door, banded with iron, was set into the wall. A heavy padlock hung from the hasp, but the hasp itself was new, the screws bright against the corroded steel of the door. A recent upgrade. I ran a gloved finger along the jamb. Fine, almost invisible scratches radiated from the lock. Picked, and not long ago.

They hadn’t bothered to re-lock it behind them. Arrogance, or a belief that no one would be fool enough to follow them into this wet, dark throat of the city.

I was that fool. I eased the door open, a sliver of darkness yielding to a deeper darkness within. The smell was immediate: damp rot, pigeon droppings, and beneath it, the faint, coppery scent of old rust. And something else. A dry, papery smell. Books.

The ground floor was a cavern, empty save for mounds of debris and the pale, watching shapes of discarded machinery shrouded in plastic. A set of wooden stairs against the far wall led up. The flashlight beam strobed again from above, painting the ceiling with fleeting, geometric shadows. Voices, muffled by distance and the drumming of rain on the roof, filtered down. Two of them. A low, methodical exchange.

I took the stairs one at a time, placing my weight along the outer edge where the wood was less likely to groan. The clarity in my head narrowed to a tunnel: the next step, the next breath, the next sliver of light from above.

The upper floor was a long, open space, once an office, now a shell. Partitions had been torn out, leaving a forest of electrical conduits and pipe stubs protruding from the floor. At the far end, in a pool of light from two powerful electric lanterns set on the floor, the two men worked.

The one from the bar—broad, tailored—was scanning the spines of books stacked in precarious towers against the wall. He handled each volume with a peculiar, surgical care, using the blade of a small knife to gently separate pages, peering into the gutters. The other man was younger, leaner, with a restless energy. He was prying up sections of the wide-plank floor with a crowbar, his movements efficient, violent. The dry, papery smell was stronger here, mixed with the scent of raw, wet earth rising from the subfloor.

This was the cache. Not a single document, but a library. A history. The physical proof of every deal, every name, every whispered promise that had built the Syndicate’s power in this city. And the man with the knife wasn’t just checking for hidden compartments. He was assessing, selecting. He would take the core, the most damning ledgers. The rest—the context, the minor players, the full story—would be left behind for the city’s bulldozers, or for a carefully set fire once the demolition crews moved in.

The broom wasn’t just sweeping. It was editing.

The younger man grunted, levering up a longer board. “Nothing under this section. Just dirt and rats.”

“Keep looking,” the older man said, his voice the same flat, accentless instrument I’d heard in the bar. “The map indicated a secondary cavity. Three feet from the north wall, eight feet from the west.”

“The map’s a century old. The walls have shifted.”

“The walls haven’t shifted. Your measurements are sloppy.”

A flicker of irritation crossed the younger man’s face, a crack in the professional veneer. He didn’t like being corrected. He was muscle, but he fancied himself a technician. A dangerous combination.

I melted back into the deeper darkness near the stairhead, behind a thick, cast-iron heating column. My pulse was that heavy, deliberate thud again. I was not an obstacle to them. I was a ghost. I could slip back down the stairs, back into the rain, and they would never know. The direction was confirmed. The net was here. But knowing the shape of the trap wasn’t the same as springing it. To stop them, I’d have to become an obstacle. And two professional men, one irritated, both armed, would not see a woman in the dark as a person. They would see a problem to be solved. Permanently.

The younger man slammed the crowbar down. “It’s not here. We’ve been at this for two hours. We have the primary ledgers. Burn the rest and be done with it.”

The older man didn’t look up from the folio he was examining. “We follow the protocol. The secondary cache contains the beneficiary registries. The names behind the names. Without them, this is just accounting. With them, it is a weapon. Find it.”

The beneficiary registries. The true currency of the Syndicate. Not just who owed, but who was owed. The politicians, the judges, the police commissioners whose loyalty was purchased not with fear, but with silent, compounding interest. It was the key to the kingdom, or its destruction.

The younger man swore, low and vicious, and drove the crowbar’s claw into the floor again. This time, the wood gave with a different sound—not a splintering crack, but a hollow, echoing groan. He froze. The older man looked up, his flashlight beam joining his partner’s on the floor.

There, in the jagged hole, was not dirt, but the dull gleam of metal. A long, flat box.

A slow, satisfied smile spread across the older man’s face. It was the most chilling expression I’d seen yet. “There. Gently now.”

I watched as they lifted the box, a sealed tin case the length of a rifle, out of the floor. It was heavy. The younger man carried it to a cleared space near the lanterns and set it down with a soft thud. The older man produced a set of lock picks from an inner pocket and went to work on the simple clasp. His hands were steady, precise.

This was the moment. The core of the core was being exposed. Once it was in their hands, in that sedan, it would vanish into a vault or an incinerator, and the city’s memory would be surgically removed.

I stepped out from behind the column.

The sound of my foot settling on the gritty floor was soft, but in the tense, silent focus of the room, it was a gunshot. Both men went perfectly still. Then, as one, they turned. The younger man’s hand went to his waist. The older man simply straightened up, his picks still in the lock, and looked at me.

His eyes held the same flat evaluation they had in the bar’s mirror. But now there was a flicker—not of alarm, but of recalculations. I was no longer part of the scenery. I had inserted myself into the equation.

“This is a restricted area,” he said, his voice devoid of any inflection that could be construed as a question.

I said nothing. I let the silence stretch, let the rain on the roof fill it. I took another step forward, into the fringe of the lantern light. Let him see the damp wool coat, the wet hair plastered to my skull, the empty hands.

The younger man drew his weapon, a compact, black automatic. He didn’t point it at me, not yet. He held it down by his thigh, his body coiled. “Who are you?”

The older man sighed, a faint exhalation of disappointment. “She’s no one. A squatter. A addict looking for a dry place.” His eyes never left mine. “Aren’t you?”

It was a test. A dismissal. A woman alone in this place could only be those things. To be anything else violated the natural order he understood.

“You’re taking what isn’t yours,” I said. My voice was rough from disuse and the cold, but it didn’t waver.

The younger man barked a laugh. “Listen to her. It isn’t ours? And it’s yours?”

“It belongs to the city,” I said. “To the people who bled for it.”

The older man’s lip curled. He finally took his picks from the lock. “Sentimentality. The most useless currency of all.” He nodded to his partner. “Remove her. Quietly.”

The younger man moved forward, confident now. I was a nuisance to be cleared. He holstered his weapon, deciding hands were enough. He was bigger, younger, stronger. The logic was immutable.

As he reached for me, his hand aiming to clamp on my upper arm and spin me toward the stairs, I didn’t pull away. I stepped into him. My left hand came up, not to block his grab, but to guide it past me, while my right drove stiffened fingers into the soft hollow at the base of his throat. It was not a fight-ending blow. It was a punctuation. A shocking, breath-stealing full stop in the narrative he was writing.

He gagged, his eyes bulging, and stumbled back a step. The surprise on his face was absolute. I didn’t press the advantage. I simply stood my ground, my hands falling back to my sides.

The older man’s flat gaze sharpened. The calculation in his eyes sped up. “I see,” he murmured. He didn’t tell his partner to get up. He assessed me anew. The sodden coat, the quiet stance, the precise, economical violence. The puzzle pieces were not fitting the picture he had pre-cut.

“You are not a squatter,” he stated.

“No.”

“You followed me from De Visser.”

“Yes.”

“You work for one of the remnant families. Seeking leverage. Or revenge.” He tilted his head. “A woman. They must be truly desperate.”

He was building a new box to put me in. It was still the wrong one, but it was more dangerous. I was now a known, if minor, adversary. An asset of a defeated foe.

“I work for no one,” I said.

“Everyone works for someone.” He took a slow step to the side, placing himself between me and the tin case. His right hand drifted toward his own coat. “Even ghosts have to eat.”

The younger man had recovered his breath, his face mottled with rage and humiliation. He drew his weapon again, this time leveling it at my chest. “Enough of this. I’ll put her down, and we can finish.”

“No,” the older man said, his voice a whip-crack. The younger man flinched. “She is not a stray dog. She is a message. Sent here. To be seen.” His eyes drilled into me. “Who sent you? Van Houten? The widow Aarden? Tell me, and your death will be quick. A professional courtesy.”

He believed this was a power play. A dying faction’s pathetic attempt to show they still had teeth, even if it was just a stray set they’d found in an alley. My solitude, my gender, made any other explanation inconceivable. I was a symbol, not an actor.

I used it. I let a slow, cold smile touch my lips—a show of teeth in the currency he understood. “You’re afraid of the wrong people.”

It was the perfect spark. The younger man, his pride still bleeding, saw the smile as a final insult. He shifted his weight, his finger tightening on the trigger. “Fuck this.”

He fired.

The report was deafening in the enclosed space. But I was already moving, not away, but toward the older man, using his body as a shield. The bullet meant for me tore through the older man’s shoulder. He cried out, a short, sharp sound of pure shock, and spun from the impact.

Chaos, true and absolute, erupted.

The younger man stared, horrified, at what he had done. The older man was down on one knee, his good hand clutching his ruined shoulder, blood welling dark between his fingers. His flat eyes were wide with pain and a dawning, volcanic fury. “You idiot!” he snarled.

The younger man swung the gun back toward me, but the moment was gone. His certainty was shattered. I kicked one of the electric lanterns. It skittered across the floor, its beam cartwheeling madly, throwing lunatic shadows, and slammed into the younger man’s shins. He stumbled.

I didn’t go for him. I went for the case.

My hand closed around the cold handle. It was heavier than I expected. The older man, from his knees, made a grab for my ankle. I kicked back, my heel connecting with his wounded shoulder. He gasped, his grip failing.

I ran. Not for the stairs—the younger man was between me and them—but for the far end of the room, where the gaping windows looked out over the river.

“Shoot her!” the older man roared, his voice ragged. “Shoot her now!”

The younger man fired again, twice. Bullets whined past me, punching into brick and timber. One shattered the remaining pane of glass in the window I was heading for. The night air, cold and wet, rushed in.

I didn’t look back. I hurled the tin case through the jagged opening. It vanished into the darkness. Then I followed it.

There was no fire escape, no ledge. Just a two-story drop into blackness and the slick, angled roof of a lower annex. I hit it feet first, the impact driving the air from my lungs, and slid down the wet tiles in a cascade of broken moss and debris. I landed in a heap on a mound of soggy cardboard and refuse in a narrow alley.

Pain bloomed in my ankle, my side. I ignored it, scrambling to my feet, my eyes searching the darkness. The tin case lay a few feet away, dented but intact.

Above, a flashlight beam stabbed out of the broken window, sweeping the alley. A voice, furious, echoed down. “Find her! Find that case!”

I grabbed the handle and ran, limping, into the labyrinth of the river bend. The net was torn. The broom was bleeding. And I had stolen its teeth.

But as I vanished into the dripping darkness, the older man’s words echoed in the hammering of my heart and the throbbing in my ankle. She is a message.

He was wrong. I was not a message.

But the gunshots in the night were. And they would be heard. By the wrong people. By the right people. By everyone. The silent, surgical excision was over. Now it was a war. And I had just lit the fuse in the one hand that could not afford to hold it.

Chapter 16

Chapter 16

The river bend was a wound in the city’s flesh, a place where the old stone wept a constant, greasy damp. I moved through its arteries, my breath a ragged saw in my throat, the tin case a dead weight that pulled at my shoulder with every limping step. The ankle was a hot, tight knot above my boot. Not broken. Sprained. A luxury of pain I could not afford.

I did not head for my own rooms. That path was a known quantity, a line they would draw the moment they had the breath to draw it. Instead, I turned deeper into the maze, towards the places that even the rats considered a downgrade. The buildings here leaned against each other for support, their upper stories kissing over the narrow passages, blocking out what little light the cloud-choked moon offered. The ground underfoot was a slick paste of mud and things better not identified.

A message. The words were a cold stone in my gut, heavier than the case. He had not meant a message for me. He had meant I was a message from someone. A statement written in blood and bullets, delivered to the doorstep of the men in that warehouse. But from who? And to what end?

The only answer was the metallic taste of fear in my mouth and the throbbing echo of the gunshots. They would be hunting now. Not just the two men—the younger one with the efficient eyes, the older one with the voice of command—but others. The net was not just torn; it was enraged. A wounded animal is more dangerous than a whole one. It does not care for its own skin.

I found a recessed doorway, the wood swollen and rotten, and pressed myself into its shadow. I set the case down between my feet and listened. The city’s night sounds filtered down: the distant clang of a tram, a shout from a street over, the eternal whisper of the river. Beneath it, the closer sounds: the scuttle in the garbage, the drip of water from a broken gutter. No boots. No hushed, urgent voices.

Yet.

I looked at the case. It was a cheap thing, dented from its fall, the green paint scarred to reveal dull metal beneath. A simple clasp, no lock. The teeth of the broom. I knelt, the movement sending a fresh lance of pain up my leg, and worked the clasp. It was stiff, corroded. It gave with a reluctant screech that seemed to echo off the wet bricks.

Inside, nestled in fitted slots of faded velvet, were six syringes. They were not medical. They were heavy-gauge glass, thick-walled, with plungers of polished brass. Each was filled with a clear, viscous fluid. No labels. No markings. Beside them, lying in a separate channel, was a sheaf of papers, folded tight and sealed with a blob of red wax stamped with a symbol I did not recognize—a stylized ‘V’ intertwined with a caduceus.

I did not touch the syringes. I pried the papers free, breaking the seal with my thumbnail. The wax crumbled like old blood. I unfolded them, angling the page to catch the faint ambient glow from a distant streetlamp.

They were shipping manifests. Lists. In a neat, bureaucratic hand. Dates. Times. Dock numbers. Ship names. And names of men. Some I knew. Middlemen. Stevedore foremen. Two city aldermen. And at the bottom of each page, a signature. A florid, arrogant scrawl I knew too well: Van Houten.

The blood in my veins went still. Van Houten was not just a name. He was an institution. A man who sat on charitable boards and dined with the chief of police. His warehouses lined the commercial docks, legitimate fronts for God knew what. And here was his hand, directing the flow of these unmarked syringes through the city’s port like so much grain or timber.

A medicine? A poison? A new kind of fear, distilled into glass?

The syringes glinted dully in the dark. They were not a weapon. They were a currency. A promise. Or a threat. The kind of thing you use to buy silence, to ensure loyalty, to break a will without leaving a mark. The kind of thing a man like Van Houten would use to sweep a problem clean away. A surgical excision.

But the surgery had gone wrong. The patient had fought back. And I had stolen the scalpel.

I refolded the papers, my fingers clumsy with cold and something deeper, a dread that was settling into my bones. I slipped them into my inner coat pocket, against my ribs. The syringes I left in their case. I closed the lid. The clasp clicked shut, a final, hollow sound.

I could not run far with the ankle. I needed a hole. A temporary burrow. There was only one person in this bend who owed me a favor that wasn’t measured in coin. A debt of a different kind.

Marta.

She ran a kroeg even smaller and fouler than De Visser, a place where the air was solid with the smell of boiled tripe and despair. It was a woman’s place, mostly. The wives and daughters and worn-out mistresses of the men who drank at Jan’s. They came to Marta for a glass of something sharp, for a listening ear that asked no questions, for a back room where a person could disappear for a few hours. Or a few days.

I had once found Marta’s son, Pieter, not ten years old, being used as a runner by a pill-peddler from the eastern docks. I had not done it for her. I had done it because the sight of the boy’s thin, frightened face in that gutter had been a reflection I could not abide. But Marta had called it a debt. “One day,” she’d said, her eyes hard as flint. “You will need a door that is not watched. That day, you come to me.”

Today was that day.

I hefted the case and pushed off from the doorway. The pain was a constant companion now, a rhythm to match my limping gait. I kept to the deepest shadows, my senses stretched taut, listening for the tell-tale rhythm of a pursuit that did not belong here. Twice I froze, pressing into alcoves, as figures passed the mouth of an alley. Once a pair of drunkards, weaving and singing. Once a lone watchman, his footsteps slow and bored. Neither were hunters.

Marta’s place was in the basement of a collapsed tannery, marked only by a faded blue door below street level. A single, flyblown bulb glowed above it. I took the steps down slowly, each one a jolt. I did not knock. I turned the handle and pushed inside.

The warmth here was different from De Visser. It was closer, denser, smelling of cheap perfume, stewed tea, and unwashed hair. The low ceiling was stained brown from smoke. A handful of women sat at small tables, their postures weary, their conversations hushed. They looked up as I entered. Their eyes were not hostile, but they were not welcoming. They were the eyes of creatures who knew the value of shadows and the danger of new things.

Marta stood behind a makeshift bar—a plank laid across two barrels. She was a thin woman, all sharp angles and tight, grey bun, her face a roadmap of hard living. She was polishing a glass with a cloth that was no cleaner than the glass. She did not look up.

“We’re closed,” she said, her voice a dry rustle.

“It’s Els.”

Her polishing stopped. She looked up. Her eyes, a pale, watery blue, took me in: the mud, the torn coat, the obvious favor my ankle was doing me, the tin case. She did not ask. She gave a short, sharp nod towards a curtain of stained sacking at the back of the room.

“Pieter,” she called, not raising her voice. A boy, now thirteen and all elbows and knees, appeared from behind the curtain. “Watch the bar. No credit. Not even for breath.”

The boy nodded, his eyes wide as he looked at me. I followed Marta through the sacking into a small, square room. It held a cot, a chair, a small stove with a kettle on it, and a pervasive smell of camphor. She pointed to the cot.

“Sit.”

I sat. The relief of taking weight off the ankle was so profound it was almost a pain in itself. I set the case on the floor beside me.

Marta busied herself with the kettle, lighting the stove with a match. “The ankle?”

“Sprained.”

“You’ll live.” She rummaged in a cupboard, pulled out a bottle of clear liquid and a roll of yellowed linen. “The rest?”

“Bullets. They missed.”

She poured a generous measure of the liquid—it smelled of anise and fire—into a tin mug and handed it to me. “Drink. For the shock.” Then she knelt, without ceremony, and began to unlace my boot. Her hands were surprisingly gentle. She peeled the boot and sock off, her lips pursing at the sight of the swollen, discolored joint. She poured some of the liquor onto the cloth and began to bind the ankle, tight and expert.

“Who?” she asked, not looking up.

“Men in a warehouse by the commercial docks. They work for Van Houten.”

Her hands stilled for a fraction of a second. Then she continued winding the bandage. “Van Houten. You stick your hand into a high nest, girl.”

“They stuck it in for me. I just pulled back hard.”

“And took something with you.” Her eyes flicked to the case.

I said nothing. The liquor was burning a path to my stomach, spreading a false warmth. Marta finished the bandage, tied it off, and sat back on her heels. She looked at me, really looked, her gaze stripping away the mud and the bravado.

“You are trouble,” she stated. Not an accusation. A fact, like the weather. “The kind that brings other trouble to my door.”

“I need until dawn. Then I’m gone.”

“And then? They will be looking for a limping woman with a tin case. Every pair of eyes on the river will have a price on them by morning.” She stood, brushing her hands on her apron. “You leave the case. You leave the coat. You become someone else for a few hours.”

“I can’t leave the case.”

“Then you are a dead woman walking, and you will die in someone else’s house. My debt is for a door, not a coffin.” Her voice was flat. “What is in it that is worth your life?”

I thought of the syringes. The manifests. Van Houten’s signature. “Proof,” I said, the word tasting bitter. “Of something I don’t yet understand.”

Marta sighed, a sound of profound weariness. “Understanding is a luxury for people with safe beds.” She walked to a trunk in the corner, opened it, and pulled out a dress.
