It was a drab, grey thing, coarse-woven, the uniform of a charwoman or a factory girl. She tossed it to me. “Put this on. Your coat goes in the stove. The case goes under the cot. You will stay here, in this room. You will be Marta’s cousin, Griet, from the country, here to help clean. You have a bad foot. You say nothing unless spoken to, and then you mumble. You understand?”

I looked from the dress to her implacable face. She was right. To move as I was, was to paint a target on my back. I had to become invisible. I had to become no one.

I nodded.

“Good.” She turned to leave. “I will bring food. And news, if there is any. The walls in the bend have ears, but they also have mouths.”

She slipped back through the sacking curtain, leaving me alone.

I stripped off my coat, my fingers lingering on the pocket where the manifests lay. I transferred them to the waistband of my trousers. Then I fed my coat, piece by piece, into the hungry little stove. The wool smoldered, then caught, the smell of damp and fryer grease giving way to acrid smoke. I watched it burn. It felt like watching a skin being shed.

I put on the dress. It was rough against my skin, smelling of lye and a stranger’s sweat. It hung on me, shapeless and anonymous. I bundled my own clothes and shoved them under the cot with the case. I sat back down. I was Griet. A girl with a bad foot. A nobody.

The act of stillness was a new kind of agony. My mind raced, circling the warehouse, the gunshots, the older man’s words. She is a message. A message to Van Houten? A declaration that his quiet, surgical game was discovered? Or a message from Van Houten, a warning to someone else that had gone awry, with me as the unintended courier?

The syringes. They were the key. A product. Something to be shipped, distributed. Bought and sold. Or administered.

Marta returned with a bowl of thin stew and a hunk of black bread. She set it on the chair. “Eat.” She stood, listening to the murmur from the outer room. “They are talking. The word is on the water. There was a shooting at the old Van Houten warehouse on the commercial dock. Two men injured. A woman seen fleeing. The city watch is asking questions, but quietly. Too quietly. Van Houten’s men are asking louder. They are offering guilders for information. A lot of guilders.”

“What are they saying about the woman?”

“That she is a thief. That she took something valuable. They are not saying what.” Her eyes narrowed. “But they are saying she is dangerous. Armed. A known associate of… unsavory elements.”

A laugh, harsh and dry, escaped my throat. “They’re building the gallows before they’ve caught the fox.”

“They are telling a story,” Marta corrected, her voice low. “And in their story, you are the villain. This makes things simple for everyone. The watch can look busy. Van Houten can get his property back. And the woman… the woman can be found floating in the river, a tragic end to a life of crime. A closed case.” She looked at the bowl. “Eat, Griet. You will need your strength.”

She left again. I forced the stew down. It was tasteless, but it was hot. The simple act of eating, of being still, of wearing another woman’s skin, began to dull the sharp edge of panic. It was replaced by a cold, focused clarity.

I was in a box. The only way out was to change the story.

To do that, I needed to know what the syringes were. And I needed to tell my story to someone who could not afford to let Van Houten’s story stand.

Jan. At De Visser. He was a neutral ground, a repository of secrets. He heard everything. And he had his own codes, his own loyalties that sometimes ran counter to the men with florid signatures. He was also the only link I had to the man who had started all this—the client who had hired me to find the missing girl, the case that had led me to the warehouse. A client who had given me a name that now felt like a set-up: De Vries. A name that had led me to a warehouse full of Van Houten’s men.

I needed to know if Jan had set the snare, or if he was just another piece on the board.

But to get to Jan, I had to get out of the river bend. And to do that, I had to move before the hunt solidified at dawn.

I waited an hour. The murmur in the outer room died down. The single bulb beyond the sacking went out. Silence settled, broken only by the drip of the gutter and the occasional creak of the old building. I pulled my own clothes from under the cot. They were still damp, but they were mine. I changed back into them, the familiar weight a small comfort. I kept the bandage on my ankle. I retrieved the case.

I pushed through the sacking curtain. The main room was dark, empty except for Pieter, asleep on a pallet behind the bar. Marta sat at a table by the dead stove, a single candle guttering beside her. She was not asleep. She was waiting.

“I’m going,” I said, my voice a whisper in the dark.

“I know.” She did not look at me. “The debt is paid.”

“It is.”

“There is a way. Through the old tannery vats. It comes out in the sewer tunnel under Brouwersstraat. It is not pleasant. But it is not watched.”

I nodded. “Thank you, Marta.”

“Do not thank me. Just go. And if you are caught, you found that way yourself. You are a thief. I am a widow who runs a poor kroeg. We have never met.”

It was the only kindness left she could offer. I took it.

She led me through a low arch behind the bar, into a space that smelled of earth and ancient, rotten chemicals. She pointed to a rusted iron hatch in the floor, half-hidden by sacks. “Down. Follow the tunnel. It slopes up. The grate at the end is loose.”

I lifted the hatch. A smell of stagnation and decay rose up, thick enough to taste. I looked back at Marta. Her face in the candlelight was a stone mask.

I descended into the blackness.

The tunnel was a tube of slick brick, barely wide enough for my shoulders. I had to hunch, the case scraping against the wall. The darkness was absolute, a physical pressure. I moved by feel, one hand on the cold, wet wall, my bad foot dragging. The sound of my own breathing was deafening. Time lost meaning. There was only the slow, painful shuffle forward, the occasional skitter of something in the muck underfoot, and the crushing weight of the earth above.

Just as the air began to feel slightly less foul, a faint, grey light appeared ahead. The tunnel ended in a vertical shaft, with iron rungs set into the wall. Above, a circular grate. Street light filtered down, diluted by fog. I climbed, each rung a trial for my ankle. I pushed at the grate. It shifted, then gave with a screech of rust.

I emerged into a narrow service alley behind the breweries on Brouwersstraat. The smell of hops and yeast was a sweet relief after the tunnel. It was the dead hour, just before the first stirrings of the city’s morning. The sky was a deep, bruised grey.

I was on the wrong side of the river bend, closer to the commercial district. De Visser was a twenty-minute walk for a healthy person. For me, it would be an hour of agony and exposure.

I had no choice. I pulled my collar up, gripped the case, and began to walk.

The city in this hour was a ghost of itself. Delivery carts began to clatter over cobbles. A lone barge hooted on the river. Lights came on in upper-story windows. I kept my head down, my pace steady, my limp a part of my disguise—just another worker heading for an early shift. I avoided the main thoroughfares, sticking to the service lanes and back streets. Twice I saw pairs of city watchmen, but they were hunched against the damp, their minds on their own discomfort, not on a lone, limping woman.

As I neared the old district where De Visser sat, the character of the streets changed. The buildings grew more cramped, the smells more familiar. I was getting close. And so was the danger.

I turned the corner onto the canal where De Visser stood. The usual yellow glow from its windows was absent. The door was closed. It was too early, even for Jan’s early clients.

I approached from the opposite side of the canal, using the bridge for cover. I watched the door. Nothing moved. The whole street was quiet, sleeping off the night.

It was too quiet.

A prickle, the old instinct, ran up my spine. The silence had a quality to it. A held breath. I looked at the windows above the bar. Jan’s living quarters. Dark.

My eyes scanned the street. A delivery van parked too neatly opposite. The curtain in a first-floor window across the way, drawn but twitching. A man leaning in a doorway down the street, pretending to read a newspaper in the pre-dawn gloom.

They were here. They had anticipated me. They had drawn a circle around the only hole I had left.

Jan was either inside, a prisoner, or he was part of the circle. Either way, De Visser was a trap.

I stood frozen on the bridge, the case heavy in my hand. The sky was lightening from grey to a sickly yellow. The net was here. It had been re-knit, stronger than before. And I was standing in the open, holding the very thing it was designed to catch.

I had no hole left. No alias. No door.

The man in the doorway folded his newspaper. He looked up. His eyes met mine across the distance.

He knew.

I turned and limped, as fast as I could, back the way I had come. Behind me, I heard a low whistle. Then the sound of boots on cobbles, starting at a walk, then breaking into a run.

The hunt was no longer quiet. It was here. It was now. And it had my scent.

Chapter 17

Chapter 17

The run was a jagged, lurching thing. My leg was a column of hot gravel, each impact sending a fresh shock up into my hip. The case slapped against my thigh, a stupid, clumsy weight. The sound of boots behind me was a spreading stain on the quiet street, not a single set but multiple, converging. They had been waiting in a ring. I had walked into the centre of it, a mouse sniffing at the cheese.

I didn’t look back. Looking back was for prey that had time for regret. I cut left into an alley so narrow my shoulders brushed the damp brick on either side. It was a gutter, not a street, choked with the reek of rotting vegetables and urine. The boots echoed at the mouth, then slowed. They wouldn’t come in single file. They’d send two, hold the rest at the ends. Professional.

Ahead, the alley T-junctioned. Right led back towards the wider canals, towards light and more of them. Left dove deeper into the old quarter, a labyrinth of leaning houses and enclosed courtyards. I went left, my breath sawing in my throat. The sky above the rooftops was the colour of a bruise, yellow at the edges, purple at the heart.

I heard a shout, answered from a parallel street. They were communicating, tightening the cordon. They weren’t chasing. They were herding.

The thought was a cold stone in my gut. I was moving, but I wasn’t choosing. My path was being shaped by their presence, funneled. I needed a door. A crack. Something they hadn’t bought or boarded up.

I burst out of the alley into a small courtyard, a forgotten space between three tall gables. A single gas lamp flickered, useless in the gathering dawn. A woman was drawing water from a shared pump, her back to me. She turned at the sound of my footsteps, her eyes widening at the sight of me—a wild thing, bleeding panic. She didn’t scream. She just took a slow step back, her grip tightening on the handle of her bucket. A judgement passed in that silence: I was trouble. Women’s trouble. The kind that drew the eyes of men with boots and whistles. Her face closed, a shutter slamming down. She would not help. She would point, later, when they asked.

There was a low archway across the court. I made for it. As I passed the pump, I saw my reflection in the dark water of her bucket—a pale, sharp mask, hair plastered to my skull, eyes like holes. No wonder she stepped back.

The archway led to a covered passage, then out onto another canal. Not my canal. A quieter, poorer one. The houses here had wooden galleries stacked above the water, laundry hanging limp in the damp air. I pressed myself into a doorway, the wood rough and splintered against my back. I listened.

Boots, to the north. A steady, walking pace. To the south, silence, but it was the wrong kind of silence. The birds had stopped. I was in a pocket. The net was drawing closed.

I looked at the case in my hand. The stupid, damned case. It was the key to the lock, and the lock was my own neck. I had two choices: drop it, or keep it. Dropping it might make me less interesting. But they had seen me with it. They knew I valued it. A woman running empty-handed was just a woman running. A woman clutching a thing, a prize, was a story. They would want the story. They would peel it from me.

So I kept it.

My eyes scanned the opposite side of the canal. The galleries. A memory, old and dusty, surfaced. Years back, a job for a ship’s chandler who liked to beat his wife. She’d paid me in information, not coin, her voice a whisper in a tavern even rougher than De Visser. She’d mentioned a place, a bolt-hole used by girls who needed to disappear from men for a night or a week. A widow, she’d said. Above a cooper’s yard. The widow took no money, only asked you work for your keep. Mending nets. Scrubbing floors. A woman’s economy.

I tried to picture the chandler’s wife’s face, but all I could see was the fear in it, and the map she’d drawn in spilled beer. Two bridges down. A yard with a blue door. A green-shuttered window on the top floor.

It was a thread. The only one I had.

I moved, keeping to the shadows beneath the galleries. The sound of the boots was closer now, a coordinated sweep. They’d be checking doorways, questioning the early risers. A man stepping out to smoke would be shown a badge, leaned on. A woman. Have you seen her? The description would be accurate. They’d have it from the man on the bridge.

I found the second bridge—a narrow, hump-backed stone affair. I didn’t cross it. I went under it, into the cold, dripping dark where the water lapped against slimy steps. On the other side, I climbed back up to street level. There. A narrow passage between two houses, leading to a courtyard. And a blue door, faded and peeling.

The cooper’s yard was silent, stacked with barrels and the sweet smell of fresh-cut wood. A light was on in a ground-floor workshop. I ignored it. My eyes went to the top floor of the house attached to the yard. A green shutter, closed.

No time for stealth. I went to the side door of the house, a servant’s entrance. I tried the handle. Locked. I knocked, three sharp raps. Nothing. I knocked again, harder.

A shadow passed across the crack of light under the door. It stopped. They were listening.

“Marta sent me,” I hissed, the name of the chandler’s wife feeling like a lie on my tongue. “The nets need mending.”

A long pause. Then the sound of a heavy bolt being drawn back. The door opened a hand’s width. An eye, pale and deeply lined, peered out. It took me in—the damp coat, the wild hair, the case, the fear that clung to me like a smell. The eye didn’t widen. It just assessed.

“Marta’s dead,” the woman said, her voice a dry rustle. “Five years now.”

My heart sank. The thread snapped.

“But the nets still need mending,” the woman said. She opened the door wider. She was small, bent, wrapped in a shawl that had once been black. Her face was a net of wrinkles, but her gaze was clear and hard as river stone. “Come in. Quickly.”

I slipped inside. She bolted the door behind me, then turned. She didn’t ask my name. She looked at my leg. “You’re bleeding through your trousers.”

“It’s old.”

“It’s fresh.” She pointed a knotted finger at the case. “That the trouble?”

“Yes.”

“Not money?”

“No.”

She nodded, as if this confirmed something. “Men chasing a woman for something that isn’t money are the worst kind. Come upstairs. Don’t drip on my floor.”

I followed her up a narrow, steep staircase that groaned with every step. The house was clean but poor, smelling of soap and boiled cabbage. The top floor was a single, low-ceilinged room under the eaves, sparsely furnished: a cot, a chair, a washstand, a small stove. The green shutter was on the far wall.

“You have until full light,” she said, standing in the doorway. “They’ll do a house-to-house then. I can’t stop them. I can say I’ve seen nothing. They might believe an old woman. They might not.”

“Thank you.”

“Don’t thank me. The floor needs scrubbing. The pots downstairs need scouring. You’ll earn the hour.” She turned to go, then paused. “There’s a loose board under the cot. If that thing needs to not be found for a minute. It’s not deep.”

Then she was gone, her footsteps a soft shuffle on the stairs.

I stood in the centre of the room, the adrenaline leaching away, leaving me hollow and shaking. I listened. The yard outside was still quiet. But the city was waking up. Carts began to rumble on distant streets. A baby cried in a neighbouring house.

I looked at the case. I set it on the cot and opened the clasps. The metal was cold. Inside, nestled in grey felt, lay the device. It was all clean lines and polished brass, a series of interlocking cylinders and delicate gears. It looked like a scientific instrument, something for measuring stars or pressure. It looked innocent. It was not.

I had stolen it from a man who thought it was a key to fortune. He was wrong. It was a key to a different kind of lock. A theoretical one. A man at the university, a man with soft hands and a mind like a razor, had explained it to me in a room that smelled of dust and desperation. He called it a predictive regulator. Feed it data—tides, cargo manifests, telegraph traffic—and its gears would turn, its needles would quiver, and it would spit out a probability. A ship leaving on a certain day would meet bad weather. A certain stock would fall. A certain official would be vulnerable to blackmail on a certain date. It was a machine for seeing the weak points in the world’s weave.

The man with soft hands had wanted it destroyed. The man I stole it from wanted to sell it to the highest bidder. I had taken it for the money, and because the soft-handed man had looked at me not with lust or contempt, but with a kind of shared, grim understanding. This should not exist, he’d said. Some threads are not meant to be pulled.

Now the highest bidders were at the door. And they weren’t commercial interests. The precision of the net, the patience of it, the way they had taken De Visser without a sound—this was state work. The city’s own shadows had come to life.

I needed a new plan. I had an hour.

I lifted the loose board under the cot. A shallow space, just as she’d said, smelling of dust and old wood. I placed the case inside, closed the board, and dragged the cot back over it. It wouldn’t fool a proper search, but it would buy a moment of confusion.

Then I went downstairs. The old woman—she never gave me her name—was in the small, dark kitchen, a pile of blackened pots at her feet. She handed me a brush and a bucket of sand and lye. “Scour.”

I got on my knees. The work was mindless, brutal. The sand scraped my knuckles raw. The chemical smell bit my nostrils. But it was real. It grounded me. The old woman sat in a chair by the cold hearth, knitting something grey and endless, her needles clicking a steady, metronomic rhythm. She didn’t watch me work. She just let me exist in her space, a temporary problem to be managed.

“They’ll offer money,” she said, after a long silence broken only by the scrape of sand on iron. “For information. They’ll lean on the weak ones. The drunks. The debtors. The ones with secrets.”

“I know.”

“You have people? Anyone they can lean on?”

I thought of Jan. His face, impassive behind the bar. A prisoner or a participant. Either way, he was a pressure point. I thought of the few others who knew my face, my trade. Ghosts, all of them. But ghosts could be summoned, given form by the right threats.

“No,” I said. But it was a lie. There was always someone.

“Good,” she said, though I knew she didn’t believe me. “A woman alone is harder to crack. They have to find you first.”

I finished the last pot, my hands red and stinging. The light at the small window was stronger now, a flat, grey daylight. The hour was up.

As if on cue, a firm knock sounded at the front door. Not the side door. The official entrance.

The clicking of the knitting needles stopped. The old woman looked at me. “Attic. Now. Don’t make a sound.”

I moved, silent and fast, back up the groaning stairs. I didn’t go into the attic room. I stood on the small landing, pressed against the wall, listening.

I heard her shuffle to the door. Heard the bolt slide. Heard a man’s voice, polite, firm. City accent, but educated. Not a street-thug’s growl.

“Good morning, madam. Apologies for the early hour. City security. We’re inquiring after a person of interest. May we come in?”

“We?”

“Myself and my colleague.”

A pause. I imagined her blocking the doorway with her small, bent frame. “What person?”

“A woman. Late twenties. Dark hair. Possibly injured. She may be carrying a case.”

“I’ve seen no one. I’m an old woman. I sleep poorly, but I don’t wander.”

“Of course. But we must check, for your safety as well. She is considered dangerous.”

A soft, dry laugh. “More dangerous than two large men in my hallway? I doubt it. But check. Be quick. I have bread in the oven.”

Boots on the floorboards. Two pairs. They moved through the ground floor, opening doors. The kitchen. The workshop. Their steps were methodical, unhurried. They knew their business.

Then the stairs. They creaked under their weight. I shrank back, my hand going to the knife at my belt. It was a pathetic gesture. If they came up here, it was over.

They reached the first-floor landing. I heard a door open. A bedroom. Then another.

“What’s up here?” the polite voice asked.

“The attic. Storage. Leaks when it rains. Mind the third step, it’s loose.”

The third step groaned, loud and plaintive. One of them swore softly.

They were on the small landing. I was around the corner, in the dark of the attic room doorway. I held my breath.

The door to the attic room opened. I could see the slice of light from the window fall into the hall. I could hear them moving inside, a floorboard groaning.

“Empty,” a second voice said, younger, terser.

“Check the space.”

A moment of silence. Then the sound of the cot being dragged. My muscles coiled. They’d found it.

But the younger voice just said, “Nothing. Dust and old linen.”

They hadn’t lifted the board. They’d looked under the cot, seen nothing, and moved on. The old woman’s trick had worked. For now.

“Thank you for your cooperation, madam,” the polite voice said from downstairs. They were leaving. “If you see anything, you’ll contact the watch house on Koningstraat.”

The door closed. The bolt slid home.

I didn’t move. I waited, counting my heartbeats. After a full minute, I heard the old woman’s steps on the stairs again. She appeared on the landing, her face expressionless.

“They’re gone. For now. They’ll watch the streets. They’ll have someone on the bridges.”

“I need to get to the river,” I said. The words were out before I’d fully thought them. The river was chaos. Barges, tugs, fishermen, smugglers. It was a different city, with its own rules. It was the only place wide enough to swallow me.

“The river is two kilometres away,” she said. “Through their net.”

“I can’t stay here.”

“No.” She looked at my clothes. “You need to look like you belong. You need a reason to be moving at this hour.”

She went back downstairs. I followed. She went to a chest in the corner of the kitchen and pulled out a bundle of rough, grey cloth. She tossed it to me. It was a dress, plain and shapeless, and a stained apron. “Fish-gutter’s shift. Put it on. There’s a basket by the door. Take it. Go to the market at Sint Jansplein. It’s in the opposite direction of the river. Walk with purpose. Head down. If they stop you, you’re Griet, fetching her mistress’s order from the fishmonger. The mistress is Frau Vanderzee, three houses down. She’s deaf and mean. They’ll get nothing from her.”

I changed in the kitchen, turning my back to her. My own clothes, still damp, went into the basket under a scrap of cloth. The dress smelled of salt and blood. It was coarse against my skin. I tied the apron, tucking my knife into the waistband. I looked at myself in the dark glass of the window. A working woman. Invisible.

“The case,” I said.

“Leave it. You can’t carry it. It’s what they want. It’s what they’ll see.”

“I have to take it.”

Her old eyes hardened. “Then you’re a fool. And you’ll be caught by noon. That thing is a man’s trouble. Let it go.”

She was right. It was the logical move. Shed the weight, become a ghost. But the case was the only leverage I had left. It was the only thing between me and total helplessness. Without it, I was just a woman they wanted to silence. With it, I was a problem they had to solve.

“I’ll come back for it,” I said.

She didn’t answer. She just shook her head, a small, final motion. She knew I wouldn’t.

I took the basket and went to the side door. She unbolted it.

“The river,” she said. “You won’t get there by running. You get there by being someone else. Remember that.”

I stepped out into the courtyard. The light was full now, a damp, grey morning. I could hear the city properly awake. I adjusted the basket on my arm, felt the weight of my clothes hidden within it, the heavier weight of the choice I’d made. I lowered my head, slumped my shoulders into the weary slope of a domestic, and walked out into the street.

I turned towards Sint Jansplein, away from the water. I walked at the pace of drudgery, not flight. A cart rumbled past. A shopkeeper was taking down his shutters. I saw two men in long coats standing at the corner where my alley met the wider street. They were scanning faces. One of them glanced at me. His eyes passed over the dress, the apron, the basket, the downturned face. They moved on, looking for a limping woman in a wet coat, clutching a case.

I passed within three metres of them. The smell of their cheap tobacco cut through the morning air. I kept walking, my heart a hammer against my ribs. I didn’t look up. I was Griet. I had pots to scour and fish to fetch.

I turned the corner, putting them behind me. The market square opened up ahead, a cacophony of shouts and bartering, a crush of bodies. Safety, of a kind. I melted into the crowd, becoming one more grey shape in the morning throng.

But as I stood at a stall, pretending to examine a mackerel, I looked back the way I had come. The two men were still at the corner. They weren’t leaving. They were setting up a watch. They knew I was in the area. They were patient. They would wait, and watch, and question every woman of my general build who passed through their filter.

The net hadn’t been broken. It had just been refined. They were no longer chasing. They were sieving.

And I was in the sieve, dressed as a piece of grit, with the river shining like a mirage two kilometres to the west. I had traded a sprint for a slow, perilous filtration. I had the disguise. I had the direction.

But I had left the key to my freedom under a loose board in an old woman’s attic. And I knew, with a cold certainty that settled in my bones, that I would have to go back for it. The machine was my only coin in a world that traded only in power. Without it, I was just another woman trying to escape the grip of men who had decided she was in the way.

The hunt was no longer a chase. It was a waiting game. And I had just walked onto their board.

Chapter 18

Chapter 18

The market’s noise was a wool blanket, thick and smothering. It pressed against my ears, a wall of shouted prices and complaints and the wet slap of fish on marble slabs. I stood at the stall, the mackerel’s dead eye staring up at me, its scales dull in the grey light. My own reflection wavered in the film of its pupil: a pinched face under a linen cap, a stranger’s fear. Griet’s fear. I let my fingers, red and chapped from the canal water, brush the fish’s cold flank. A performance of consideration. A housewife’s deliberation.

All the while, my blood sang a single, shrill note: they are still there.

I had outrun the dogs, but the hunters had simply closed the gate. They were leaning against the brickwork of the bakery now, the two long coats. They had lit fresh cigarettes, the smoke a pale grey signal against the damp air. They were settled. This was no longer pursuit; it was a shift. One watched the mouth of my alley. The other let his gaze drift over the crowd, a slow, mechanical sweep. They had all day. They had the authority to stop any woman who fit the silhouette of their suspicion. My disguise was my cage. To run now would be to paint a target on my back. To stay was to let the sieve shake me slowly, inevitably, into their hands.

The fishmonger, a woman with forearms like dock ropes and a face carved from salt, narrowed her eyes at me. “You going to buy that or pray over it?”

I flinched, the Griet in me rising to the surface with a practised cower. “Sorry. It’s… the price.”

She snorted, turning to shout at a boy lugging a crate of mussels. The moment broke. I stepped back, melting into the eddy of bodies. The basket on my arm was an anchor, pulling me down into the role. I moved with the current of the crowd, letting it carry me deeper into the square, away from the western edge where the river lay, away from the men. Every step was a betrayal of my need. The machine called from its hiding place like a second heartbeat, a dull, insistent throb of loss beneath my ribs.

I needed a different kind of currency. The kind Jan traded in at De Visser: information, favour, the subtle leverage of a debt. But Jan was across the city, and the only capital I had here was the fiction of Griet, and the few coins in the pocket of this dead woman’s dress.

I bought a loaf of black bread. I bought two withered turnips. Each transaction was a stitch in the tapestry of my cover. I spoke in the flat, clipped tones of the district, my head bowed. I became a moving part of the market’s machinery, a cog greased with mundane worry. I saw other women like me—or like Griet—their faces closed as shutters, their movements economical. They navigated the press of bodies with a weary expertise, a silent sisterhood of labour. We were ghosts in plain sight, seen only for our function: to buy, to cook, to clean, to birth. To be sieved from the world of consequence.

A hand closed around my upper arm.

It was not a rough grip, but it was absolute. The fingers, thick and strong, pressed through the wool of my sleeve. My heart stopped, then slammed against my breastbone like a bird in a chimney. I did not look up. I became a stone. A piece of grit.

“You. Girl.”

The voice was a man’s, bored, official. It came from above and to my left. I made my eyes wide and blank, turning my head just enough. It was one of the long coats. His partner was still at the corner, a sentry against the brick. This one had waded into the stream to pluck out a likely piece of flotsam. Me.

“Meneer?” I let my voice go thin, reedy.

His face was long, sallow. He had the eyes of a clerk, not a thug; this was worse. This was procedure. “Where do you live?”

“Kuipersteeg, meneer. Number five.” The lie was ready, a flat on the other side of the square, not near my alley.

“What’s in the basket?”

I showed him. Bread. Turnips. The mackerel I had not bought. He poked at them with a gloved finger.

“You’re out early.”

“The master likes his breakfast by seven, meneer.” I kept my gaze on the second button of his coat. Subservient. Uninteresting.

“See a woman running this way? Earlier. Limping. Looked… out of place.”

I allowed a flicker of confusion to cross my face. A servant’s comprehension was slow, carefully doled out. “Running, meneer? No. Only the fish carts. And the boys.” I risked a glance up, then quickly down. “Is there trouble?”

He studied me. His look was not of suspicion, but of assessment. Was I worth more questions? Was my face the one on the leaflet in his pocket? I felt the weight of the disguise like a suit of lead. He was not seeing me. He was seeing class, gender, utility. He was deciding if this particular tool was the one he needed.

A shout went up from a cheese stall—an argument over weight. The market’s noise, which had dampened around us, surged back. His partner at the corner gestured impatiently. Their net was wide; I was one small fish. The calculus of his boredom tipped.

He released my arm. “Move along. Don’t dawdle.”

“Yes, meneer.” I bobbed my head, a shallow, instinctive dip, and scurried into the thicker crowd, my basket clutched tight. The heat of shame that flooded me was not Griet’s. It was mine. The performance had been perfect because it was not a performance at all. It was a language he expected me to speak, and I had spoken it fluently. I had traded on my own invisibility. It had saved my skin and hollowed out my chest.

I did not stop shaking until I was three streets over, in the lee of a chandlery, the smell of tar and rope a solid thing in the air. I leaned against the wet brick, breathing hard. The false victory of my escape curdled in my gut. I had not outsmarted them. I had merely conformed, perfectly, to the narrow channel they allowed for women like me. The disguise worked because it was a prison they recognised.

The machine. It was the only thing that ever made a space for me that was my own. A space of gears and pressure, of cause and effect I controlled. It was a man’s world, that world of mechanics, but within the cradle of its logic, my hands were the only ones that mattered. Without it, I was back in the sieve. Griet. Els. Names for the same commodity.

I had to go back.

The logic was as cold and clean as a steel bearing. The attic room was in the heart of the area they were watching. They would be looking for someone fleeing, not returning. They would be looking for a limping woman in a coat, not a maid with a basket, finishing her marketing. The old woman, Mrs. de Wit—she was a variable. Had they questioned her? Would she give me up for a guilder? Or was her solitude its own kind of fortress?

I had no other move. The river was a taunt. Freedom was a locked door, and the key was under a warped floorboard in a room that smelled of dust and mothballs.

I spent the day in a purgatory of slow movement. I walked the perimeter of their net, a rat learning the dimensions of its trap. I saw them stop two other women. One, older, they let go quickly. The second, younger, closer to my build, they questioned at length. I watched from the doorway of a haberdashery, my face half-hidden by a row of hanging aprons. I saw the woman’s shoulders hunch, her hands twist. She had nothing to tell them. Her fear was answer enough. They let her go, but the message was etched in the air: we can do this. All day. To any of you.

The afternoon bled into a damp, early twilight. The market packed away. The shopkeepers pulled their shutters closed. The long coats were replaced by two others, a changing of the guard. The sieve remained.

This was my window. The dogged patience of the day shift would be at its lowest ebb. The night shift would be fresh, their eyes sharper for movement in the growing dark. But the transition itself was a moment of blurred responsibility.

I emptied my basket of its pathetic provisions behind a rain barrel. The bread, the turnips. I kept the shawl. My heart was a cold, hard knot. I was not returning to hide. I was returning to steal back a piece of myself.

The alley, when I turned into it, was a canyon of shadows. The few windows were already glowing yellow, curtains drawn. I walked with Griet’s tired trudge, head down, a servant returning from an errand. The sound of my own footsteps was too loud. I passed the doorway where I’d slept. I passed the pump. The silence was a held breath.

The door to Mrs. de Wit’s building was unlocked. The stairwell was dark, smelling of cabbage and mildew. I climbed, each creak of the wood a thunderclap in the hush. On her landing, I paused. No light showed under her door. No sound came from within.

My attic door was as I’d left it. I slipped inside, into the absolute black. I stood for a full minute, listening to the blood roar in my ears, waiting for a footstep on the stair, for the old woman’s cough. Nothing.

I crossed to the far wall on my knees, my hands sweeping the rough planks. My fingers found the familiar warp, the nail head. I pried it up.

The emptiness beneath was a physical shock.

I plunged my hand into the space, sweeping it left, right. Dust. Splinters. Nothing. My breath hitched. I felt along the adjacent board, pried it up too. More nothing.

A cold deeper than the canal water seeped into my bones. She had found it. Mrs. de Wit. While I was playing Griet in the market, she had been up here, prying up my floorboards.

The loss was so total it felt like a vacuum. I knelt in the dark, my hands flat on the empty hole. The machine. My designs. The only proof that I was more than the world’s grit. Gone.

A floorboard creaked behind me.

I froze. The door was still closed. The sound had come from within the room.

“I wondered when you’d come back.” The voice was Mrs. de Wit’s, but stripped of its quaver. It was dry, precise. A click, and the warm glow of an oil lamp flooded the attic. She stood by the dormer window, a shape in a dark dress, the lamp in her hand. Her eyes were not the milky, vague things I remembered. They were sharp, and they held me pinned.

“You took it,” I said. My voice was flat. Dead.

“It’s safe,” she said. She didn’t move. “A remarkable piece of work. The tolerances. The spring mechanism. It’s not a locksmith’s tool. It’s a musician’s instrument.”

She understood it. The realisation was a second blow.

“What do you want?” I asked, still on my knees. The power balance had not just shifted; it had inverted. She was the holder of my soul. I was the supplicant.

“I want to know who you are,” she said. “The men at the corner are city watch, but the questions they’re asking… they’re not for a petty thief. They’re for someone who has offended a powerful man. The machine offends in a particular way. It is a key to places you are not meant to go.”

I said nothing. The silence stretched. She took a step forward, the lamplight carving deep gullies in her ancient face.

“My husband was a clockmaker,” she said, her gaze drifting to the window, to the darkening sky. “A genius with tiny things. He made a singing bird for the daughter of a burgomaster. A masterpiece. They paid him in praise and a pittance. When he asked for what it was worth, they laughed. They broke his hands with a hammer so he could not make another for their rivals. He spent the last twenty years of his life watching his own genius leak away into pain and regret.” Her eyes snapped back to mine. “I know the smell of a man who fears a clever thing in the wrong hands. Who is he?”

“Visser,” I whispered. The name was ash in my mouth. “Hendrik Visser.”

A slow nod. “The shipping magnate. A man who owns doors, and the right to open them.” She placed the lamp on the floor between us. “So. You are a woman with a machine that opens doors, and Hendrik Visser wants you stopped. That makes you either the greatest fool in the city, or the only interesting person to have darkened my doorstep in a decade.”

“I need it back,” I said, the plea naked in my voice.

“Why? To run? They will catch you. A woman alone, with a singular tool… you are a beacon. You have already learned you cannot run as a woman. You can only… dissipate.” She gestured to my Griet clothes. “That is a slow death.”

“What choice do I have?”

She smiled then, a thin, papery thing. “You stop being the grit in the sieve. You become the hand that shakes it.” She bent, with a stiffness that was not feigned, and from the shadows by her feet, she lifted the leather case. My case. She held it out. “Take it.”

I stared, unmoving. It was a trick. It had to be.

“Your design has a flaw,” she said, her voice clinical now. “The third actuator spring. It’s under too much tension. It will fatigue, then snap, at the worst possible moment. My husband’s notes are in the case. A modification. It will hold.”

I rose, my legs numb. I took the case. The familiar weight was a shock of warmth. “Why?”

“Because Visser broke my husband’s hands,” she said, her voice like iron filings. “And I am too old to break his neck. But you… you have the key to his doors. And I have just given you the means to turn it in the lock without it breaking in your hand.” She stepped closer, her sharp eyes boring into mine. “Running is what they expect. It is the woman’s option. Hiding is what they expect. What they do not expect is for the mouse to turn and gnaw through the ropes of the winch. To collapse the trap on the trapper.”

The idea was so vast, so terrifying, it left no room for breath. Not escape. Not hiding. Attack.

“I am one woman,” I breathed.

“You are a woman with a machine that opens anything,” she corrected. “Visser’s power is built on locks. On vaults. On private rooms and secured ledgers. On the assumption that what is his stays his. You are a solvent to that assumption.” She turned back to the window. “The men outside are looking for a fugitive. Be something else. Be a ghost in his machine. Be a rumour in his walls. Make him afraid of his own locks.”

She was handing me not just my tool, but a new direction. A war, not a flight. The false victory of my escape was truly shattered now, blown apart not by capture, but by this offer of a different, more terrible path. The gender dynamics I had used as a shield were now a weapon she was placing in my hands. They would not look for a saboteur in a maid’s dress. They would not suspect the quiet, fearful gender of audacity.

“They will hurt you,” I said. “When they find you helped me.”

She did not look back. “Let them try. I have been waiting twenty years for a reason to make a noise.”

I looked down at the case. It was no longer just my freedom. It was a weapon. A declaration. I had walked onto their board as a piece to be captured. Mrs. de Wit was telling me to flip the board and set it on fire.

“Thank you,” I said, the words utterly inadequate.

“Go out the back,” she said, pointing to a small, hinged panel in the wall I had never noticed—a servant’s pass-through from a time when the attic housed live-in help. “It leads to the roof of the washhouse, then down to the next street. Avoid the sieve.”

I moved to the panel, my mind churning with the seismic shift of it all. As I pushed it open, letting in a gust of damp, free air, she spoke once more.

“Els.”

I turned. She had never asked my name.

“Make it sing,” she said.

Then she blew out the lamp, plunging herself, and the attic, and the ghost of her husband, back into the dark.

I slipped through the opening, onto the steep, wet tiles. The case was solid in my grip. Below, in the street, I could see the glow of a watchman’s lantern, a tiny star in the gloom. They were still sieving. Still waiting for the woman who ran.

I turned my face away from the river, away from the west. I looked east, towards the old city, where the gabled houses of the wealthy crowded together, where Hendrik Visser’s offices overlooked his docks.

The hunt was no longer a waiting game. It was a different game entirely. And I had just been given the only piece that mattered.

I began to climb down, away from the light, into a different kind of dark.

Chapter 19

Chapter 19

The dark between the gables was a different substance than the dark of the attic. It was cold and wet and alive with the drip of condensation from rusted gutters, the scuttle of rats in the valleys of the tiles. I moved across the roof of the washhouse, the leather case a hard, accusing weight against my ribs. The tiles were slick with a film of city grime, each step a negotiation with gravity. Below, the watchman’s lantern bobbed, a patient, predatory glow. They were sieving the streets, but the rooftops of this district were a forgotten lattice, a world for cats and chimney sweeps and now, for me.

I dropped from the washhouse eaves into a narrow alley that smelled of wet stone and cat piss. The case thumped against my side. I paused, listening. The murmur of the search was a street over, the calls of the watchmen muffled by brick. They were looking for a fugitive, a desperate creature fleeing west towards the river and the promise of a boat. They were not looking for a woman walking with purpose east, into the heart of the merchant belt, carrying a gentleman’s valise.

That was the first lie. The dress was the second.

I found a recessed doorway, deep enough to swallow the light from the distant streetlamp. My fingers, numb with cold and the aftermath of the attic’s revelation, worked at the clasps of the case. They opened with a soft, oiled click. Inside, nestled in fitted velvet, was not a spy’s kit of lockpicks and poisons, but a uniform. A maid’s uniform, severe and grey, with a white apron folded crisp as a legal document. Beneath it, a pair of sensible, low-heeled shoes. At the very bottom, wrapped in a scrap of silk, was a bone-handled paring knife, its edge honed to a wicked gleam. A tool, not a weapon. A woman’s instrument.

I shed my own coat, the damp wool reeking of the river and fear. The cold bit through my blouse. The maid’s dress was coarse, a rough-spun wool that scratched at my neck and wrists. It was several sizes too large, meant for a broader, stouter frame. Mrs. de Wit’s frame, twenty years ago. I used the belt to gather the excess fabric, pulling it tight until it was merely shapeless, not engulfing. The apron strings I tied in a precise, efficient bow at the small of my back. The shoes were a close enough fit. I bundled my own clothes into the case, tucked the knife into the deep pocket of the apron, and left the case in the shadowed corner of the doorway. An offering to the alley. A discarded piece of a previous game.

I stepped out of the alcove.

The transformation was not in the mirror, for there was none. It was in the space I now occupied in the world. My shoulders, which had been set in a defensive hunch, now rounded forward naturally, my chin dipping towards my chest. My stride, which I forced into a long, ground-eating pace, shortened to quick, efficient steps. The change was not posture alone. It was a yielding, a making of oneself small within the available space. I had observed it a thousand times in the women who served at De Visser, in the scullery maids who darted through the grand houses like mice. It was an art of invisibility through expectedness.

I turned onto the broader street. A pair of watchmen, their greatcoats dark with rain, stood under the eaves of a chandler’s shop. One held a lantern aloft, its light carving hollows in their faces. I did not avoid them. I did not meet their eyes. I kept my gaze lowered to the cobbles three paces ahead, my hands clasped loosely in front of me, the very picture of a servant on an errand, hurrying home before the curfew bell. The light washed over me. I felt it like a physical touch.

“You there,” one called, his voice bored.

I stopped, my body tensing not into flight, but into a respectful, fearful stillness. I turned my head just enough, keeping my eyes downcast. “Meneer?”

“Out late. Whose house?”

“Mevrouw van der Linde, meneer. On the Keizersgracht. Sent for more candles.” My voice was higher, softer, stripped of the gravel I cultivated at the bar. It was the voice of a girl who expected to be chastised.

He grunted. “Van der Linde’s lot are always burning midnight oil. Counting their guilders, I’d wager. Get on with you. Straight home, mind.”

“Yes, meneer. Thank you, meneer.”

I scurried past, the subservience a sour taste in my mouth. Their attention, that searching, suspicious beam, had slid right off the grey wool and the white apron. I was furniture. I was part of the city’s background noise. The power of it was dizzying, and utterly repellent. This was the shield. This meekness, this expected fear, was the cloak that rendered me unseen. Mrs. de Wit had not given me a disguise. She had given me a social blind spot.

The rain had settled into a persistent drizzle. The grand houses of the eastern canals were monuments of shadow and gilded light, their windows glowing behind thick curtains. I knew Hendrik Visser’s house. Everyone did. It was not the oldest, but it was the most aggressively maintained, a statement in red brick and white trim that glared across the water at its rivals. His offices, however, were separate. A merchant prince did not soil his home with ledgers and the smell of anxious clerks. His domain was a narrow, four-story building wedged between a sugar refinery and a shipping insurer’s, overlooking his own docks. It was a fortress of commerce, and it was where he would be, even at this hour, on the eve of a major shipment. He would be counting, like van der Linde, but his count would be of crates and timetables, of profit and risk.

I approached from the rear, through a service lane choked with delivery carts and empty barrels. The back of the building was a plain façade of soot-stained brick, punctuated by a heavy oak door and a line of high, grimy windows. The ground-floor window was lit. The kitchen, or a clerk’s late-night pantry. A single window on the third floor also showed a faint, steady glow. An office. His office.

The lock on the service door was a heavy, iron thing, but it was oiled for daily use. The paring knife was too light, too short. I needed a different key. I retreated into the deeper shadows, my mind working. A maid did not pick locks. A maid had legitimate reasons to be near doors.

I waited. The drizzle seeped through the coarse wool, finding my skin. Time stretched, measured in the distant bells of the Westerkerk, each chime a hammer fall on the city’s quiet. Then, the service door opened. A young kitchen boy, yawning, shuffled out with a bucket of slops. He trudged towards a midden heap at the end of the lane, his shoulders slumped with tiredness.

The door swung shut behind him, but not fully. It caught, perhaps on a warped floorboard inside, leaving a gap of two inches.

I moved as he emptied his bucket, the sound masking my steps. As he turned back, rubbing his eyes, I was already at the door, my body angled as if I had just emerged. I held it open for him, a small, silent courtesy.

He blinked, surprised to see another servant in the gloom. “Oh. Thanks.”

I offered a tight, tired smile, the universal language of the overworked. “Long night.”

“Aren’t they all,” he muttered, and slipped past me inside.

I followed him in, pulling the door quietly closed. The expectedness. He did not question my presence because my presence was expected. The kitchen was a cavern of shadows, lit by the dying embers in a vast hearth. The air was thick with the smells of old grease, boiled cabbage, and yeast. A scullery maid dozed on a stool by the sink. The boy disappeared through another door.

I did not hesitate. A maid in a house had purpose, direction. I walked through the kitchen, past the sleeping girl, and into a stone-flagged passageway. A set of narrow, twisting stairs led upwards, the wood worn smooth in the centers by generations of servants’ feet. I took them, my soft shoes making no sound. The building had a different breath at night, a hollow, creaking stillness. From behind a door on the second floor, I heard the scratch of a pen and the soft sigh of a man. A clerk, working late.

The third-floor landing was darker, carpeted in a worn runner that swallowed my steps. The light seeped from under a single door, a bar of yellow against the gloom. I pressed my ear to the wood. Silence. Then, the crisp, definitive sound of a ledger being closed. A chair scraped.

I retreated to a shallow alcove where a broom and dustpan were kept. I took the broom, leaning it against the wall as if I’d just been interrupted. The performance was everything. The door opened.

Hendrik Visser filled the doorway, not with bulk, but with presence. He was a tall man, lean as a whip, his hair steel-grey and swept back from a high forehead. He wore a dark blue house coat over his shirt and trousers. In one hand he held a ledger, in the other, a glass of what looked like port. His face was all angles and calculation, the eyes pale and distant, as if permanently fixed on a horizon line of profit and loss. He did not see me, tucked in my alcove. He looked down the hall towards the stairs, his mind clearly on other things.

He turned back into his office, leaving the door ajar. I heard the clink of decanter on glass, the rustle of papers.

This was the moment. The knife in my apron pocket was a cold comfort. This was not a task for a blade. It was a task for a ghost, for the unremarked gender that cleaned up after the decisions of men. I left the broom. I stepped to the door and pushed it open just enough to slide through.

The office was a testament to controlled power. It was not large, but every object in it was of the finest quality and utterly functional. A vast oak desk, clear but for a brass lamp, an inkstand, and the ledger. Shelves of identical ledgers lined one wall. A large window, now black with night, would by day command a view of the Visser docks. A fire guttered low in the grate. He stood with his back to me, staring into the embers, the glass of port held loosely in his fingers.

He spoke without turning. “I said the figures could wait until morning, Pieter.”

His voice was dry, precise. It was the voice of a man accustomed to being obeyed, to the world arranging itself according to his ledgers.

I did not answer. I let the silence stretch, a different quality of silence than the obedient silence of a clerk.

He turned. His eyes found me, and for a fraction of a second, there was no recognition, only a flicker of irritation at the interruption. Then the irritation cooled into something harder, more analytical. He looked at the grey dress, the apron, the downcast eyes I no longer bothered to fully maintain.

“You are not from this house,” he stated. It was not a question.

“No, meneer.”

“How did you get in?”

“The kitchen door was open, meneer.”

He took a slow sip of his port, his gaze never leaving me. He was assessing me as he would a piece of dubious cargo. “You are the one they are sieving for. The woman from the river.”

I said nothing. The admission was in my presence, in the quiet defiance of my posture.

A faint, cold smile touched his lips. “And you come to me. In a maid’s dress. That is either remarkably stupid or remarkably bold. Which is it?”

“I came to ask a question, meneer.”

He raised an eyebrow, amused. “A question. You disrupt my night, invade my premises, and you wish to conduct an interview? You have the advantage of me. You know my name. I do not know yours.”

“Els.”

“Els.” He rolled the name on his tongue, finding no flavor there. “And your question, Els?”

I met his pale eyes fully then, dropping the pretense of the maid. “The Zuiderkroon sails on the morning tide. Its manifest is sealed by your office. What is in hold seven?”

All amusement vanished from his face. It became a mask of polished stone. He set his glass down on the desk with a soft, definitive click. “You are not a customs inspector. You are not a rival merchant. You are a barmaid with a talent for trouble. Why would you care about a ship’s hold?”

“Because people who ask about it end up in the river.”

“An occupational hazard of curiosity in this city.” He walked around his desk, not approaching me, but moving to stand behind it, placing its solid bulk between us. A tactical repositioning. “Hold seven contains machine parts. Lathes. For the Swedish ironworks. The paperwork is in order.”

“Machine parts do not require a man like Maarten to guard them. Or a man like De Wilde to kill for them.”

The name De Wilde did what I hoped it would. A tiny fissure appeared in the stone. A minute tightening around his eyes. He knew the name. He knew the kind of work it implied.

“You are speaking of things beyond your understanding,” he said, his voice lower. “You have stumbled into a game where the pieces are not yours to move.”

“Mrs. de Wit gave me the piece,” I said.

Now the stone cracked. His hand, resting on the ledger, went very still. The name of his former master’s widow, spoken in this context, was a key turning in a lock he thought sealed. He looked at me anew, not as a nuisance, but as a genuine anomaly. A variable his ledger had not accounted for.

“Agatha,” he breathed, the word a mix of disbelief and dawning fury. “She sent you here. In this… costume.”

“She told me to make it sing.”

He was silent for a long moment, his gaze turning inward, computing. I could see the connections being made behind his eyes: the sieving, the missing woman, the valise from his old master’s house, the question about the hold. He arrived at the conclusion with the speed of a master accountant.

“You have the case,” he said.

“I had it.”

“And you think coming here, asking me this, will achieve what? My confession? A change of heart?” A bitter laugh escaped him. “You are a message from a ghost, nothing more. Agatha seeks to haunt me from her attic. It is a pathetic gesture.”

“What is in the hold, Visser?”

He leaned forward, his palms flat on the desk. “Power, you foolish woman. Pure, condensed power. Not guilders, not influence. The kind of power that rewrites maps and topples councils. The kind of power your mind cannot even encompass. It is a transaction. A delivery. The culmination of twenty years of patience and planning. And it is none of your concern.”

“It became my concern when your men tried to put me in the water.”

“A regrettable overzealousness. You were seen with the wrong person. You became a loose thread. Loose threads are clipped.” He straightened. “But you are not a thread. You are a splinter. And you have walked into the heart of the wood. Pieter!”

His call was sharp, clear. From down the hall, I heard the clerk’s door open, footsteps.

The performance was over. The shield of the dress was paper against the direct gaze of its master. I did not run for the door. He would expect that. I moved towards the window.

“There is no exit there,” Visser said, a note of contempt returning. “It is three floors to the cobbles.”

The footsteps were in the hallway now. Not just Pieter’s. Heavier, quicker. More than one.

I reached the window, fumbling with the latch. It was stiff, painted shut. I yanked at it, the cheap fabric of the dress straining at my shoulders.

The office door opened. Pieter, a thin man with ink-stained fingers, stood there, blinking. Behind him were two others—not clerks. They had the broad shoulders and empty eyes of warehouse enforcers, men who handled crates and problems with equal brute force.

“Take her,” Visser said, his voice returning to its dry, managerial calm. “Quietly. The same as the others.”

The two men stepped forward. I stopped fighting the latch. My hand dipped into my apron pocket and closed around the bone handle of the paring knife. I did not draw it. I turned to face Visser, my back to the dark window.

“Machine parts,” I said, my voice flat. “For the Swedish ironworks.”

He nodded, a dismissive gesture. “Precisely.”

The enforcers were three paces away. Pieter hovered in the doorway, a spectator.

“Then you won’t mind,” I said, “if I check the paperwork.”

With my free hand, I swept the brass desk lamp off the desk. It was heavy, full of oil. It struck the floor with a crash of glass and metal, and the oil, fed by the wick, erupted in a whoosh of hungry flame across the dry wood and the scattered papers.

Visser’s composure shattered. “You mad bitch!”

The enforcers halted, startled by the sudden wall of heat and light that bloomed between us. The fire, fed by ledger pages, leaped towards the shelves. Pieter cried out.

In the chaos, I turned back to the window. This time, I did not fumble. I drove the heel of the paring knife’s handle against the latch, once, twice. The old paint cracked. I wrenched it open.

Cold, wet night air rushed in, battling the heat of the fire. Below was a sheer drop to a brick-paved courtyard.

“Stop her!” Visser roared, beating at the flames on his desk with his house coat.

One of the enforcers lunged through the growing fire, his hand grabbing for my arm. I twisted, and the coarse sleeve of the maid’s dress tore in his grip. I brought the paring knife up, not to stab, but to slash—a sharp, shallow cut across the back of his grasping hand. He snarled, recoiling more in surprise than pain.

It was enough. I put a foot on the windowsill, the city’s breath cold on my face. I did not look down. I looked at Hendrik Visser, his face a mask of fury illuminated by his own burning ledgers.

“I’ll be checking the manifest,” I said.

Then I pushed myself out, not down, but across the narrow gap to the roof of the adjacent sugar refinery. It was a leap of faith and desperation, a span of five feet of black, rainy air. The apron billowed, the grey dress flapped. My feet hit the sloped, lead-clad roof, skidded on the wet metal, and I fell forward, my hands scrabbling for purchase. I slid, a cascade of noise, until my toes jammed against a runoff gutter.

I lay there, gasping, the smell of burning ledger and my own fear sharp in my nose. Shouts echoed from Visser’s window. They would not follow this way. They would take the stairs, flood into the lane. I had minutes.

I pushed myself up. The refinery roof was a complex terrain of vents and chimneys. I moved across it, away from the light and the noise, my body aching, the maid’s dress torn and smudged with soot. The shield was gone, burned away in that office. They knew my face now. They knew I had the knowledge, and the will to act on it.

But I knew something, too. His reaction was the confirmation. Hold seven of the Zuiderkroon did not contain lathes. It contained the reason for everything. The source of the power he coveted. And he was terrified I would reach it.

I dropped from the refinery roof into a different alley, my body a symphony of new pains. The curfew bell began to toll, a deep, sonorous knell that rolled over the city. The sieving would intensify. They would be looking for a maid, now. A specific maid, with a torn dress and a knife.

I had no weapon but the truth. No shield but the audacity he thought my gender incapable of. The board was on fire. And I was not a piece. I was the hand that held the match.

Somewhere to the west, in the vast, dark throat of the river, the Zuiderkroon waited for the tide. It was no longer just a ship. It was a vault. And I had just blown the lock off its secret.

I melted into the maze of the old city, a grey ghost against the older stones, the taste of smoke and port on the air behind me. The hunt was no longer me fleeing them. It was me hunting the truth, and they knew it. The game had changed again. And for the first time, I saw a flicker of fear in the eyes of the man who kept the ledgers.

Chapter 20

Chapter 20

The rain had gentled to a persistent, probing mist that seeped through stone and spirit alike. It beaded on the wool of my stolen jacket—a man’s work coat, rough and smelling of fish-oil and sweat, taken from a line in a cramped courtyard. The grey dress was a sodden bundle weighted with a brick and dropped into a canal. I was a dockworker now, cap pulled low, shoulders hunched in a way that felt less like disguise and more like the natural curl of a creature expecting a blow.

The knowledge was a cold, hard stone in my gut. Hold seven. The source. It was no longer an abstract suspicion in a ledger; it was a physical place, a thing I could touch, if I could only reach it. De Vries’s fear had been a hotter, brighter thing than any anger. It had been the pure, incandescent terror of a man who sees the linchpin of his world wobble. That terror was my compass now.

But the city had tightened like a fist around a suspected infection. Patrols moved in pairs, their boots a synchronized threat on the wet cobbles. The usual nocturnal murmur of the docks—the furtive deals, the loading of contraband by lantern-light—was absent, smothered under the weight of official attention. The Zuiderkroon was a silhouette against the lesser dark of the river, a black cut-out studded with a few guarded pinpricks of light. A vault, indeed. And every eye on the waterfront was a lock.

I needed a key. Not a physical one. A distraction. A shift in the board that would pull their gaze away from the ship and the river.

Jan’s face surfaced in my mind, the way he’d pushed the jenever across the zinc. Payment expected later. Usually coin. But he dealt in other currencies: information, silence, the careful direction of a certain kind of clientele. He was a pivot. Men like De Vries saw the De Visser as a useful gutter, a place where the dregs of their schemes could be quietly disposed of. They never considered that the gutter had ears, and a memory, and its own slow, patient currents.

The tavern was quieter than I’d ever seen it. The usual crowd of off-shift refinery men and canal-boat pilots was thin, made up of the most desperate or the most connected. A tense silence hung in the air, thicker than the smoke. Conversations were whispers. The clink of a glass was a minor explosion.

Jan was polishing the same spot on the counter with a grim, methodical energy. He didn’t look up as I approached, my work-boots making soft, wet sounds on the floorboards. I didn’t take a stool. I stood at the end of the bar, where the light was poorest.

“They’re pulling men from the east-end refineries,” I said, my voice low, stripped of anything that could be called feminine. It was just a rasp of fatigue. “Double watches on the company warehouses. Not for theft. For containment.”

His polishing didn’t falter. “Bad weather makes for poor security.”

“This isn’t the weather. It’s a leak. A specific one. They think it’s headed for the river.”

Now he looked up. His eyes, small and dark in the forest of his beard, held no surprise. Only a weary calculation. He saw the man’s coat, the cap, the posture. But he looked past it, to the set of my jaw, the unblinking directness of my gaze. He saw me. “The river is a big place. Easy to drown in.”

“Not if every skiff and lighter is being stopped and searched. Not if the Port Master’s men have orders to detain anything female and alone.” I let a hint of the old voice through, a sliver of steel. “They’ve painted a target. And they’re holding the brush.”

He sighed, a sound like gravel shifting. He poured two fingers of jenever into a tumbler and slid it toward me. “And you’re here to… what? Wait for the tide?”

“I’m here to change it.” I left the glass untouched. “The Zuiderkroon sails with the morning tide. Before then, they’ll move the cargo from hold seven. It’s too hot to stay. They’ll try to land it somewhere quiet, upriver or along the coast. They need a distraction. A bigger fire to fight.”

A flicker in his eyes. Not interest. Caution. “Starting fires is a young man’s game. It leaves ashes. And bodies.”

“This city runs on a slow fire, Jan. It’s just contained in furnaces and ledgers and the bellies of ships. All I’m suggesting is… redirecting the fuel. For an hour. Maybe two.” I leaned forward, my palms flat on the sticky wood. “The east-end refinery men. They’re restless. Pulled from their shifts, told it’s for security, but they see the company guards getting bonus pay. They smell the inequality. It’s a tinderbox.”

He laughed, a short, humorless bark. “And you want me to strike the match? I run a tavern, not a revolution.”

“You run a clearing house. For grievances, and for solutions. You know the foreman, Pieter. The one with the debt to the company store. The one whose brother vanished after asking about pension funds.” I’d heard the stories here, in this room, over months. The low, resentful murmurs I’d filed away as background noise. Now they were a ledger of a different kind. “He’s proud. And angry. If he thought the company was using him and his men as pawns, to cover something that would make the directors richer while his family starves… his pride wouldn’t let him stand for it.”

Jan studied me for a long moment. The guttural murmur of the tavern seemed to recede. Here was the calculation: my value against my danger. I was danger incarnate now. But I was also a vector. A way to direct the coming explosion away from his walls, and toward the interests of the men who looked down on this place and everyone in it.

“What do you offer?” he asked finally. Not in coin.

“The truth. The reason for the panic. Not rumors. Not ‘company business.’ The fact that De Vries is terrified of what’s in that hold. That he’d beggar every man in this room to protect it. Give Pieter that. Not as a plea. As a weapon. Let him decide what to do with it.”

He nodded, slowly. A decision made. “The jenever is on the house tonight. For certain customers. The talk will be… pointed.” He met my eyes. “But you won’t be here to see it. And if you are caught, you fell from a roof. You stole that coat. You have never spoken to me.”

“Understood.” I finally took the glass, tossed the liquor back. It was fire and caraway and the taste of a bargain with the devil you know. “One more thing. I need to get to the Zuiderkroon. Not at the docks. Out in the stream. After the distraction starts, but before they can move the cargo.”

He stared at me as if I’d asked him to fetch the moon. Then a slow, grudging respect dawned in his face. “Madness.”

“The only currency they don’t account for.”

He jerked his head toward the back. “There’s a boy. Klaas. He’s simple. He loves the river. He knows every current, every eddy, like a lover’s skin. He takes a skiff out at night sometimes, to be away from the noise. He moors it at the old, broken pier behind the tannery. No one bothers him.” Jan’s gaze was flint. “If you bother him, if you bring trouble to his boat, I will find you. And what I will do will make De Vries look like a gentleman.”

“Where will he be?”

“Where he always is. Looking at the water.”

The broken pier was a skeleton of blackened wood reaching a few futile feet into the dark water. The tannery stench was a physical wall, a reek of chemicals and decay that made the eyes water. And there, sitting on the very end with his legs dangling over, was a hunched figure.

Klaas was maybe sixteen, with a round, placid face and eyes that held the river’s constant movement. He didn’t startle as I approached. He just turned his head, his expression open as a child’s.

“You’re not supposed to be here,” he said, his voice soft. “It’s my place.”

“Jan sent me.”

At Jan’s name, his suspicion eased. “Oh. Do you like the water?”

“I need to use it. I need to get to a ship out in the stream. The big one, with the three masts.”

“The Kroon,” he said, nodding. “She’s heavy in the water. Sitting low. They’ve been loading her all week. At night.” He said it as simple fact, with no implied secrecy.

“Can you take me? In your skiff? When… when there’s a lot of noise and light from the refineries?”

He considered this, looking back at the river. “The current is tricky there. It pulls toward the dredging channel. You have to know how to use the eddy behind the chain-boat. Otherwise you’ll be swept past.” He stood up, brushing off his trousers. “I can show you the eddy.”

He led me to a tiny, tar-painted skiff tied to a rusted ring. It was beautifully kept, the oarlocks greased, the single pair of oars worn smooth by his hands. He didn’t ask why. He didn’t ask about the man’s coat, the tension in my shoulders. The river was his language, and my request was a phrase he understood.

We waited. The mist turned to a light, cold rain. The city sounds were muffled, distant. Then, a change. Not a sound at first, but a vibration in the air, a shift in the quality of the darkness to the east. A faint, orange glow bloomed above the rooftops, not the steady light of refinery furnaces, but a leaping, hungry illumination.

Shouts followed, distorted by distance into a single roar of anger. A bell began to clang, not the slow curfew bell, but the rapid, frantic alarm of the fire watch. The distraction was not a small fire. It was a riot.

Klaas looked toward the glow, his face calm. “That’s the noise,” he said.

“Yes.”

He untied the skiff, held it steady as I climbed in, my movements clumsy with a tension he did not share. He pushed us off with a single, expert shove against the pilings and slid the oars into the water. We moved into the blackness of the river, the sounds of the city fracturing into distinct waves of noise: the alarm bells, a deeper, booming crash that might have been a door being forced, the rising, unified chant of men’s voices.

Klaas rowed with an effortless, rhythmic grace, his eyes on the water, reading its surface like a page. He angled us not directly toward the Zuiderkroon, but upstream, letting the current catch us. The ship grew from a silhouette to a vast, wall-sided mass. Lanterns swung on its deck, revealing clusters of men at the rails, looking toward the commotion on shore. The distraction was holding.

“The eddy,” Klaas whispered, and with a few strong, silent pulls, he swung the skiff into a patch of strangely still water behind a moored dredge barge. The current wanted to pull us past the ship’s stern, but here, we hung, bobbing gently, hidden in the deeper shadow of the barge. We were amidships, just aft of the great, dark bulge of the hull that was hold seven.

A loading door was set into the ship’s side, about ten feet above the waterline. It was closed. A Jacob’s ladder, used for pilots, was rolled and secured beside it. My way in.

“I can’t hold here long,” Klaas murmured. “The eddy spits you out.”

I looked at the slick, black hull, at the small, distant rectangle of the door. It was impossible. A leap from a moving skiff, a climb with no proper handholds. The audacity he thought my gender incapable of. It was all I had.

I stripped off the heavy work coat, let it fall into the bilge. Underneath, I wore dark, close-fitting clothes I’d taken from the same line—a man’s shirt and trousers, rolled at the cuff. I tied the cap tighter on my head.

“When I jump, you go,” I said. “Don’t look back. Don’t wait.”

Klaas nodded. “The river will take you, if you fall.”

“I know.”

I waited for the skiff to rise on a small swell. As it neared the apex, I stood, my balance precarious. I focused on a rusted bolt-head just below the loading door. A tiny, imperfect handhold. I coiled my legs and pushed off.

The world became a violent lurch of motion. My fingers scraped across cold, wet iron, caught the bolt. My body slammed against the hull, the impact driving the air from my lungs in a silent gasp. I hung there, my feet scrambling against the slime-covered plates. I heard the soft, quick dip of Klaas’s oars as he pulled away, swallowed by the dark.

Above, the sounds of the ship were clearer: shouts of confusion, orders bellowed to keep men at their stations, to ignore the shore. They were looking landward. Not down.

I began to climb. It was a spider’s progress, finding cracks between plates, the heads of rivets, the lip of a scupper. Each grip was a gamble, each upward shift of weight a potential plunge into the waiting river. My muscles screamed, burned from the roof-fall, the running, the cold. The loading door was a mere arm’s length away. I stretched, my fingers finding the cold iron handle. It was dogged shut from the inside.

I hung there, pressed against the ship, the reality of my position crashing down. I was a barnacle on a leviathan. I had no tools, no plan beyond this point. The truth was inside, but I was stuck to the outside of its vault.

Then, a vibration through the iron. A deep, metallic clunk from within the hull, right behind the door. Machinery engaging. A winch. They were moving something. Preparing.

The handle above my head began to turn.

I froze, my blood turning to ice in my veins. The door was opening outward. It would swing out and knock me into the water. I had seconds.

I pushed sideways along the hull, fingers clawing for any purchase. I found the edge of the rolled Jacob’s ladder, the rough hemp of its ropes. As the loading door creaked open, a wedge of yellow lantern-light slicing the darkness, I hooked my arm through the ladder’s rungs and pulled my body tight against its roll, making myself part of the ship’s furniture.

The door swung open fully. A man stood silhouetted in the light, his face in shadow. He leaned out, looking toward the shore, the reflected glow of the distant riot dancing on his features. It was one of De Vries’s clerks, the nervous one from the office.

“Madness,” he muttered to someone inside. “The whole east end is alight. He says it changes nothing. We proceed.”

“The lighter is ready?” another voice asked from within.

“Almost. The crane is secured to the load. We just need the all-clear from the bridge. It’s chaos up there. Half the crew are gawping at the flames.”

He stayed there, leaning on the door frame, a sentry bored with his post. I hung beside him, close enough to see the stubble on his jaw, to smell the tobacco on his breath. My arm ached with the strain of holding still. A drop of water from my hair fell, landing on his hand resting on the door.

He looked at his hand, then up, directly at the rolled ladder.

His eyes met mine.

For a heartbeat that spanned an eternity, we stared at each other. His were wide with the slow-dawning horror of the impossible. A face from a burning office, now clinging to the side of his ship. A ghost made flesh, and dripping wet.

His mouth opened to cry out.

I moved. My free hand shot out, not for the door, but for the lantern he’d set on the deck just inside. I snatched it by its wire handle and swung it in a short, brutal arc. The glass shattered against the side of his head. The flame guttered and died on the wet iron. He crumpled without a sound, slumping over the door sill.

I hauled myself over him, into the hold.

The smell hit me first. It was not the smell of a ship’s hold—not of damp timber, or burlap, or spices. It was dry, and cold, and carried a faint, acrid tang of ozone and something else, something profoundly mineral, like the air deep inside a cave. The space was vast, lit by a few suspended lanterns. And it was not filled with crates or machinery.

It was a laboratory.

Long wooden tables ran the length of the hold, bolted to the deck. They were laden not with cargo, but with glassware: beakers, retorts, distillation coils of a complexity I’d only seen in engravings of alchemists’ dens. There were scales with weights of polished brass, notebooks stacked in precise piles, and jars containing powders and liquids of unnatural colors—a vivid, sickly green, a purple so deep it was almost black.

In the center of the space, secured to the deck by heavy iron brackets, was the source of the power he coveted.

It was a machine, but unlike any I had ever seen. It stood about eight feet tall, a construct of polished copper, glass cylinders, and thick, insulated cables that snaked away to a bank of monstrous chemical batteries. At its heart, contained within a glass sphere held in a cradle of copper claws, was a rock.

It was not a gem. It was rough, about the size of a man’s fist, and it glowed. Not with heat or reflected light, but with its own internal, pulsating radiance. A cold, blue-white light that throbbed like a slow, steady heartbeat. It cast sharp, dancing shadows through the hold.
