With each pulse, a faint, hair-raising crackle whispered from the machine, and the air tasted sharper, more metallic.

This was it. Not gold, not spices. Not a weapon, in the conventional sense. It was a source. A battery of unimaginable potency. De Vries wasn’t just cornering a market; he was trying to corner the future itself. The refineries, the shipping lanes, the political favors—they were all just the scaffolding to protect and exploit this. A piece of a star, fallen to earth and caged in glass.

“You.”

The voice came from the far end of the hold, near a secondary hatch. A man stepped from behind a bank of instrumentation. He was tall, gaunt, dressed not in sailor’s gear but in a stained leather apron over a fine shirt. His hands were long-fingered, delicate, stained with chemical burns. His eyes, magnified by thick spectacles, were fixed on me with a fanatic’s intensity. This was the mind, the alchemist. The keeper of the stone.

He held a heavy glass stirring rod like a club. “You are the disruption. The variable in the equation.”

I stood, the broken lantern handle still in my hand, water pooling at my feet. I was inside the vault. The truth was before me, humming with alien energy. But I was not alone with it.

“He’s terrified of anyone seeing this,” I said, my voice echoing strangely in the cavernous, charged space. “Why? What does it do?”

The alchemist’s lips stretched into a thin, proud smile. “It redefines possible. It is not a thing that does. It is a thing that allows. Light without flame. Motion without steam. A silent, clean, endless pulse of power.” His gaze was rapt upon the glowing stone. “He is terrified because he is a merchant. He understands control of a commodity. He does not understand a paradigm. And paradigms are harder to kill than people.”

Footsteps pounded on the deck above, urgent. Shouts filtered down. They had found the clerk at the door.

The alchemist’s smile vanished. He looked from the stone to me, and his fanaticism curdled into a cold, pragmatic fear. “You have seen it. That is enough. You must not leave. The equation must be balanced.”

He raised the glass rod and started toward me, not with a fighter’s grace, but with the grim determination of a man protecting his life’s work.

I backed away, my eyes scanning the tables. The truth was here, dazzling and terrible. But the vault had become a cage. And the keeper of the truth was coming to silence the witness, his spectacles gleaming in the cold, pulsating light of the stone that promised a new world, and had made this one a hunting ground.

Chapter 21

Chapter 21

The glass rod came down not with a fighter’s swing, but with the terrible, efficient arc of a butcher’s cleaver. It was meant to split my skull, to end the equation. I threw myself sideways, the movement a wet, graceless stumble. The rod whistled past my ear and struck the edge of the iron table with a crystalline pang that shivered up into my teeth. A long, wicked shard spun away into the shadows.

The alchemist—no, the technician, the keeper—blinked behind his spectacles, surprised by the resistance of matter. His world was one of theory and luminous potential. Mine was one of grease-slick floors and the immediate, pressing need to not have my brains spilled.

I came up with the broken lantern handle reversed in my grip, the splintered end a crude spike. The water from my clothes had spread around me, a dark halo on the stone. He adjusted his grip on the rod, the remaining length a jagged club. His eyes flickered past me, to the stone. Its light pulsed, a slow, patient heartbeat that painted the cavern in waves of cold blue and deep, velvety shadow. It was watching. Or it felt like it was watching.

“You don’t have to do his killing,” I said, my voice low, measured against the hum. “A man like Visser understands a knife, a debt, a threat. This?” I jerked my chin toward the stone. “This is a god. You don’t serve it by being his street-corner thug.”

His lips, thin and bloodless, tightened. “You reduce it to metaphor. It is not a god. It is a key. And he holds the door.” He took a step, his leather apron creaking. “You are a loose thread. You unravel things.”

Above us, the pounding became a thunder. A heavy object slammed against the vault door—a ram, maybe, or a shoulder fueled by panic. The clerk’s body wouldn’t hold them long. The keeper heard it too. A tic jumped beneath his eye. His sanctuary was breached. The pure math of his work was now corrupted by the messy variables of men with clubs and questions.

He lunged again. This time the rod came in a sideways sweep, aiming for my ribs. I didn’t try to block it; glass against iron-shod wood was a losing proposition. I stepped inside the swing, into the space his fanaticism had left empty. The world narrowed to the smell of his chemicals—sour ammonia, sharp ozone—and the magnified, frantic blue of his eyes behind their lenses.

I drove the splintered end of the lantern handle into the meat of his thigh.

It was not a deep wound. The wood was rough, not sharp. But it was a shock. A violation of his clinical space. He gasped, a sound of profound indignation, and stumbled back, his free hand going to the puncture in his leather apron. A dark bloom spread, not from the shallow stab, but from the broken glass still embedded in the rod’s end, which had raked his own palm. He stared at the blood, his own, with a kind of detached curiosity.

The door above groaned. A sharp, metallic crack echoed down the shaft. They were through the lock.

The keeper’s gaze snapped from his hand to me, to the stone, then to the spiral stairs. The calculus in his head was visible: the specimen, the witness, the invaders. The specimen was paramount. The witness was a contaminant. The invaders were a tide.

He made his choice. With a snarl that held more anguish than anger, he hurled the glass rod at my head. I ducked, and it shattered against a bank of copper coils with a sound like falling chimes. Then he turned and ran, not for the stairs, but deeper into the vault, toward a smaller, iron-banded door I hadn’t seen, half-hidden behind a rack of ceramic batteries humming with captured light.

He was abandoning his creation. Or he was trusting it to be a harder secret to steal than a man.

I didn’t chase him. The truth wasn’t in the man. It was here, thrumming in the air, making the fillings in my teeth ache. The door at the top of the stairs burst open. Boots on metal, shouting. Visser’s voice, raw with a fury that was mostly fear, cut through the din. “Find her! Kill her! Nothing else matters!”

They thought the stone was just a jewel, a prize. They didn’t feel its pulse in their marrow.

I looked at the stone on its pedestal. The alchemist’s words echoed. A thing that allows. It was a heart. A silent, beating heart for a machine not yet built. To take it would be to claim a kingdom. To leave it was to leave a weapon in the hands of a frightened, greedy man.

But I was not a thief of kingdoms. I was a finder of secrets. And a secret seen is a secret halved.

I moved. Not toward the stone, but toward the nearest worktable. My hands, slick with canal water and now a smear of the keeper’s blood, swept across its surface. I wasn’t looking for tools. I was looking for notes. The scrawl of genius. I snatched up a leather-bound journal, its pages thick with diagrams and cramped, frantic script. I shoved it inside my coat, against my ribs. It was a cold, hard rectangle of truth.

A heavy glass beaker stood next to a rack of vials. I grabbed it, hefted its weight. Then I turned and threw it, not at the men now clattering down the stairs, but at the stone’s pedestal.

It was a terrible shot. I wasn’t aiming to hit the stone itself—the thought of what that impact might do curdled my blood—but at the base. The beaker struck the iron support with a crash. Glass and a clear, viscous fluid exploded. The fluid hit the cables snaking from the stone’s base. For a heartbeat, nothing.

Then the stone flared.

It wasn’t a pulse. It was a silent, blinding detonation of blue-white light. It etched the bones of my hands against my eyelids. It bleached the color from the world, turning everything to stark negative: black shadows, white edges. A wave of force, not heat but a profound, shoving pressure, slammed into my chest. It lifted me off my feet and threw me back against the iron table. My breath left me in a grunt of pure shock.

The men on the stairs cried out—not in pain, but in primal terror. The light was wrong. It was a color that had no business in the belly of a barge. It was the light of a cold, new sun.

The glare died as suddenly as it had bloomed, collapsing back into the stone’s core, but its afterimage danced in my vision like a ghost. The hum had changed. It was higher now, strained, a wire vibrating near its breaking point. Thin tendrils of smoke rose from the scorched cables at the pedestal’s base.

I pushed myself up, my ears ringing in the new silence. The keeper’s door hung open, a black rectangle. He was gone. On the stairs, three of Visser’s men were frozen, clutching the railing, their faces masks of superstitious dread. They had come down for a woman, a fight in the dark. They had found a silent, screaming star.

Visser stood behind them, at the top of the stairs. The unnatural light had hollowed his face, made his eyes into dark pits. He wasn’t looking at me. He was staring at the stone, now flickering erratically, like a heart in arrhythmia. His treasure was broken. Or waking up. He couldn’t tell which, and the not-knowing was a torture worse than any threat I could pose.

“Get it,” he whispered, the command carrying in the dead air. “Get it under cover. Now.”

His men didn’t move. They were sailors, used to wind and wave and the tangible violence of knives. This was a witchcraft that stripped them of their courage.

I used their paralysis. While they stared at the dying light, I moved toward the only other exit: the keeper’s door. I kept low, behind the tables, my wet clothes making no sound on the stone. The journal pressed against my side, a stolen bible of this new, terrible faith.

One of the men, younger, with a weal of a fresh burn across his cheek, finally shook himself. He saw my movement. “Boss,” he hissed.

Visser’s gaze snapped to me. The fear in his eyes crystallized into a hatred so pure it was almost clean. I had seen. I had broken. I had to be erased, not just killed, but unmade from the history of this moment. “Her,” he said, the word flat and final. “Just her.”

The younger man came down the last of the stairs, drawing a long, tar-stained knife from his belt. The other two, galvanized by a command they understood, followed. The stone was forgotten. I was a human problem, and they had human solutions.

I reached the small door and slipped through, into darkness. A narrow corridor, hewn from the ship’s timbers, smelling of damp rot and chemical runoff. I pulled the door shut behind me. There was no lock, only a simple latch. It wouldn’t hold them.

I ran. The corridor sloped upward, following the curve of the hull. Pipes hissed overhead. Somewhere ahead, water dripped in a steady, maddening rhythm. My breath sawed in my throat, the earlier exhaustion now a lead weight in my limbs. The coat was a sodden beast on my shoulders, the journal a slab of stone.

The door behind me crashed open. Boots pounded in the passage. They didn’t shout. They were hunting now, and silence was the tool.

The corridor ended at a T-junction. To the left, a faint, greasy yellow glow suggested a working area, maybe the keeper’s private lab. To the right, pitch black, and a cooler draft carrying the smell of the canal. The way out.

I went right.

The darkness was absolute. I kept one hand on the rough, wet timber of the wall, the other extended ahead, feeling for obstacles. The draft grew stronger. Ahead, a faint, grey suggestion of light—a grating, perhaps, or a hatch. Freedom, or a trap.

A shape loomed out of the dark to my left, a deeper blackness. I sensed more than saw the movement, the shift of air. I dropped to a crouch. The knife swished through the space where my neck had been, striking the wall with a solid thunk. The younger man. He’d found a parallel passage, or he knew the maze better than I did.

I didn’t rise. I scissored my legs out, sweeping his feet from under him. He went down with a choked curse, the knife skittering away. I was on him before he could draw breath, my knees pinning his shoulders, my hands finding his face in the dark. I didn’t punch. I pressed my thumbs hard into his eyes, not to blind, but to panic. He bucked, gagging, his hands clawing at my wrists. I held the pressure for a count of two, then released and rolled off, snatching for the knife. My fingers closed on the hilt.

He was scrambling back, whimpering, his hands to his face. I stood, the knife in my hand. The other two were close; I could hear their cautious steps in the main corridor.

The grey light was stronger now. Ten paces ahead was a heavy iron grate, covering a circular opening in the hull. Moonlight and the reflected gaslights of the dock filtered through, illuminating a small, empty space—a dry storage locker, now abandoned. The grate was held by four large, rusted wingnuts.

I sheathed the knife in my belt and went to work. The first nut turned with a shriek of metal. The second was frozen solid. I drew the knife again, using the pommel as a hammer, beating at the stubborn wing until it sheared off with a crack. The noise was catastrophic in the confined space.

“Here!” a voice shouted from behind me. A lantern’s beam swung around the corner, blinding me.

I threw my weight against the grate. With two bolts gone, it held for a second, then buckled outward with a groan. I squeezed through the gap, splinters tearing at my coat, and tumbled out onto a narrow, slimy ledge just above the waterline of the barge. The canal was a black mirror below, stinking and cold.

The locker was on the barge’s far side, away from the dock. Across the water, maybe fifteen feet, was the pitted stone wall of the opposite quay, the foundations of a warehouse. No ladder. No rope.

A head thrust through the grate behind me. A hand, holding a pistol, wavered in the moonlight.

I didn’t think. I jumped.

The water was a physical agony, a shock that stopped my heart for a beat before it began hammering against my ribs. It was not the clean, cold shock of the sea. This was a thick, gelid stew of filth and winter. My coat, the journal inside it, became an anchor. I fought the instinct to thrash, to gasp. I kicked, driving downward, away from the surface light, following the curve of the barge’s hull.

The pistol shot, when it came, was a muffled, watery thump. The bullet tore a line of bubbles past my shoulder, spent and harmless. I swam, my lungs burning, my hands scraping against slimy stone and submerged timber. I counted. Ten strokes. Twenty.

My head broke the surface in the deep shadow between the barge and a neighboring coal hulk. I sucked in air that tasted of tar and decay, the sound masked by the lap of water against hulls. I treaded water, listening.

Voices, angry and confused, carried from the deck of Visser’s barge. A lantern beam swept the water where I’d jumped. It didn’t reach my hiding place.

I had to move. The cold was leaching the strength from my bones, turning my muscles to sluggish clay. I let the current, sluggish as it was, carry me down the length of the coal hulk. At its stern, I found a mooring chain, a thick, slimy serpent leading up to the quay. I hauled myself out, hand over hand, the links biting into my frozen palms. Every pull was a separate universe of effort.

I collapsed onto the cobbles behind a stack of empty crates, vomiting a gout of canal water. My body shook uncontrollably, a violent, ceaseless tremor. I was a thing of mud and ice. But the journal was still there, a solid block of damning evidence pressed against my shuddering heart.

I needed to get off the docks. Visser’s men would be fanning out, checking every shadow. He couldn’t let me live now. I had seen the core of his power, and I had made it sick. I had the book. I was a vector for his ruin.

Pushing myself up, I staggered into the maze of alleys behind the warehouses. I moved from pool of darkness to pool of darkness, a creature of the in-between places. The grand houses of the Herengracht felt a world away. Jan’s bar was a memory of warmth I might never feel again. I had nowhere to go. The stone’s cold light was seared behind my eyes, and the keeper’s words were a chant in my head. A thing that allows.

I understood now, in my marrow. Visser wasn’t just a thief or a profiteer. He was a man trying to bottle lightning, to sell silence, to own the future. And he would burn the present to the ground to keep it.

A figure detached itself from a doorway ahead, blocking the narrow passage. Tall, lean. Not one of Visser’s bruisers. The moonlight caught the fine wool of his coat, the pale gleam of his face.

Rafael.

He didn’t speak. He simply looked at me, dripping and shaking in the alley filth. His expression was unreadable. Not surprise. Not pity. A kind of weary recognition, as if he’d been waiting for me to arrive at this exact, desperate point.

“They’re hunting you,” he said, his voice quiet. It wasn’t a question.

I nodded, my teeth chattering too hard to form words.

He glanced back the way I’d come, then at the sky, judging the hour. “He’s pulled men from the watch. Paid for silence. The docks are his for the next few hours.” He stepped closer. The scent of him—soap and clean linen—was a brutal contrast to the reek of the canal on my skin. “You have something he wants. More than your life.”

I clutched my coat tighter over the journal. “You work for him.”

“I work for the city,” he corrected, his tone flinty. “He is a cyst upon it. I thought he could be lanced with careful pressure. You have taken a sledgehammer to it.” He sighed, the sound full of a profound fatigue. “Now it will rupture. And poison everything.”

He reached out a hand. Not to take the journal. To steady me. His grip was firm, warm through my soaked sleeve. “You cannot go to ground. He owns the ground. You cannot run. He will close the gates.”

“Then what?” I managed, the words slurred with cold.

He looked at me, and in his eyes I saw not the polished functionary, but the man who walked the knife-edge between law and survival. “You give him what he wants. But not on his terms. You force the rupture on your ground. You show the cyst to the light.”

He was talking about a confrontation. A public one. It was madness.

“He’ll kill me,” I said.

“Yes,” Rafael agreed, without hesitation. “He will try. But he is a merchant. He understands an auction. A trade.” His gaze dropped to the bulge of the journal beneath my coat. “You have the manifest. You offer it back. In exchange for a hearing. In front of people he cannot buy, or cannot afford to kill.”

“Who?”

“The men whose money built that stone,” he said. “The syndicate. They are not all in his pocket. Some are… nervous. About his methods. About the attention.” He meant the deaths. The disappearances. The clerk with his throat cut. Me, hunted through the streets. “They meet tomorrow night. At the Koning van Denemarken. A private room. He will be there, to reassure them.”

The most powerful men in the city. I would be a mouse walking into a council of cats.

“They’ll side with him,” I said. “Money sides with money.”

“Perhaps,” Rafael conceded. “But they are also men who hate a mess. You are a mess. He has failed to clean you up. That makes him look weak. And in their world, weakness is the only sin.” He released my arm. “It is the only move you have left, Els. Stand in the light where he cannot simply make you vanish. Make your accusation. Show your proof. And hope the stink of his failure is stronger than the glitter of his gold.”

He was offering me a path, but it was a path across a tightrope, over a pit of wolves. He was not my ally. He was a man steering a crisis toward an outcome that might, incidentally, save his city.

“Why?” I asked, the single word hanging in the cold air between us.

For the first time, something like emotion touched his face. A faint, grim twist of the lips. “Because I, too, have seen what that stone can do. Not its light. What it does to the men who hold it. It makes them see people as… variables. To be balanced out.” He met my eyes. “I find I do not care for the equation.”

He turned and gestured for me to follow. “There is a place you can dry out. A safe place. For tonight.”

I hesitated. This could be the trap. The neat, final solution.

But the cold was in my core now. The trembling was becoming a dangerous lethargy. I had no other cards to play. I was a variable he had not yet balanced.

I took a step, then another, following Rafael’s silhouette into the deeper darkness of the alley. The journal was a lead weight. The stone’s dying light was a ghost in my mind. And ahead, in the warm, wood-paneled room of a gentlemen’s club, a different kind of machine awaited—one of money, power, and polished lies. I would have to walk into its gears and trust that my truth, ugly and dripping with canal water, was a wrench solid enough to jam them all.

Chapter 22

Chapter 22

The safe place was a room above a printer’s shop in the Jordaan. It smelled of ink, cheap paper, and the ghost of old tobacco. Rafael left me there with a key, a loaf of black bread, and a warning that felt like a seal being set on a tomb.

“Be at De Witte Olifant at eight tomorrow. Ask for the Zeeuwse Kamer. They will bring you up. Do not be late. Do not be early. Wear something that does not look like you pulled it from a bin.”

He did not wait for an answer. The door clicked shut, and I heard the solid turn of a second lock in the hall. A courtesy, or a containment. The room held a narrow bed, a washstand with a chipped pitcher, a single chair. A window looked out onto a dark canal, the opposite house leaning close enough to touch. It was a cell with a view.

I set the journal on the bed. My coat steamed in the room’s dry heat, releasing the smell of the city’s underbelly. I peeled it off, then the wet wool of my sweater, my fingers clumsy and numb. The cold had migrated inward, settling in the marrow of my bones. I poured water from the pitcher—it was clean, shockingly so—and scrubbed at my face, my neck, the grime of the warehouse and the canal. The face in the small, spotted mirror was a stranger’s. Pale. Eyes too dark, set in bruise-colored sockets. A mouth that had forgotten any shape but a line.

I was a mess. That was my value. My only weapon.

I slept then, a black and dreamless plunge, and woke to the gray, pre-dawn light seeping between the buildings. The trembling was gone, replaced by a hollow, resonant stillness. I felt like a shell. I ate some of the bread. It was dense, sour. It tasted of nothing.

The clothes Rafael had implied I needed were folded on the chair. A simple, dark wool dress, slightly too large, but clean. Stockings. Sturdy, polished shoes that pinched at the heel. The uniform of a respectable, anonymous woman. Dressing in them was an act of camouflage. I tucked the journal into a deep pocket sewn inside the skirt, its weight a familiar pendulum against my thigh.

The hours until eight were a desert. I sat by the window and watched the canal come to life. A barge moved past, loaded with barrels. A woman across the way shook a rug from a window. A cat picked its way along a gutter. Ordinary life, moving with the blind, persistent rhythm of ants. I was outside of it, a speck of grit about to be dropped into a watchworks.

De Witte Olifant was on the Herengracht, a fortress of wealth behind a severe, classical façade. Its windows were tall, clean, and blank. I arrived as the bells were striking eight, my borrowed shoes silent on the swept cobbles. The door was opened by a man in a black coat whose eyes took me in, dismissed me, and then flickered with a faint surprise when I spoke.

“The Zeeuwse Kamer.”

He stepped back without a word. The interior was a hushed world of dark wood, worn leather, and the sweet, dusty smell of old money. Portraits of stern men in ruffs looked down from the walls. The carpet swallowed sound. My presence here was a dissonant note, a stain on the harmony. The man led me up a wide staircase, down a corridor lined with closed doors, and stopped before one of polished oak. He knocked once, opened it, and gestured me inside.

The room was a library. Shelves of leather-bound books rose to the ceiling. A fire crackled in a marble hearth, and the air was thick with the scent of cigar smoke, brandy, and beeswax. Four men were arranged in high-backed chairs. They did not rise.

Visser sat nearest the fire, one leg crossed over the other, a glass of amber liquid in his hand. He looked like he had been carved from the same wood as the furniture, polished to a hard, impermeable sheen. His eyes met mine, and there was nothing in them. No anger, no recognition. I was a document to be reviewed.

The other three I knew by reputation, if not by sight. De Wit, the shipping magnate, his face a roadmap of burst capillaries. Van Dijk, who owned half the warehouses north of the IJ. And Scholten, the banker, small and precise as a watchmaker, his fingers steepled before his lips.

A fifth chair, straight-backed and unwelcoming, sat empty facing them. My place.

Rafael stood by the window, his back to the room, looking out at the canal as if the proceedings were of no interest to him. He was the guarantor of this audience. The moderator. Or the executioner.

“Sit,” said De Wit, his voice a gravel rumble.

I sat. The chair was hard. I kept my hands in my lap, over the hidden weight of the journal. I did not look away from them.

Visser took a slow sip of his drink. “Gentlemen. This is the woman I mentioned. Els. Formerly in my employ. She has, I am given to understand, some… grievances she wishes to air.” He said the word grievances as if it were a childish thing, a tantrum.

“We are busy men,” Van Dijk said, his gaze cool and assessing. “This is highly irregular.”

“It is,” Visser agreed. “But Rafael felt it prudent. To avoid… further public disturbances.” He shifted his eyes to me. “Speak, then. You have stolen from me. Conspired against me. You have made accusations to the wrong people. Here are the right people. Say your piece.”

It was a masterful opening. He had framed me before I could utter a word. A thief. A hysteric. I was not a victim bringing a charge; I was a problem being managed.

The silence stretched. The fire popped. I could feel the pressure of their collective gaze, a physical weight. They were not cats. Cats had passion, curiosity. These men were stones. Smoothed by the flow of money and power until all that remained was inertia and mass.

I did not speak to Visser. I looked at De Wit, then Van Dijk, then Scholten.

“My name is Els. I was a diver for Mijnheer Visser. I retrieved things for him from the mud. Two months ago, I retrieved a stone. A blue stone. From the wreck of the Zwarte Zee.”

Scholten’s steepled fingers twitched. Van Dijk’s eyes narrowed a fraction.

“I was instructed to bring it directly to him. I did. He took it to his workshop. That night, a man named Pieter, his metallurgist, was killed. His throat was cut. The official report called it a robbery.” My voice was flat, dry. It echoed strangely in the opulent room. “I was curious. I went back to the wreck. I found a body, wedged in the timbers. A man, dressed in fine clothes, not sailor’s gear. He had been dead a long time. He had a journal on him.”

Visser’s expression did not change. He swirled his brandy.

“The journal was written by a Portuguese alchemist. He was on the Zwarte Zee. He called the stone the Luz do Abismo. The Light of the Abyss. He wrote about what it could do. What it was for. It wasn’t treasure. It was a key. A battery. It could store… potential. Energy. A man’s life force. His will.” I was parroting terms I barely understood, but the words from the journal had seared themselves into my mind. “The alchemist was afraid. The ship’s captain, a man named Hendriks, had taken the stone. He believed it could make him powerful. Invincible. The last entry says the alchemist heard screaming from the captain’s cabin. Then the ship struck the reef and went down.”

I pulled the journal from my pocket. The water-stained, leather-bound book was a stark, ugly object in the refined room. I held it up. “It’s all here.”

“A fascinating story,” Visser said, his tone bored. “A shipwreck mystery. And you believe this… fairy tale… connects to me?”

“Pieter connected it,” I said, turning to him at last. “He was trying to understand the stone. You were helping him. Then he was dead. And you had the stone locked away, experimenting. You found a use for it. Not to store a life. To drain it.”

Van Dijk leaned forward. “Drain it.”

“To power a machine,” I said. “A loom. A prototype. One that doesn’t need a river, or steam, or a room full of women to run it. One that runs on… this.” I tapped the journal. “On will. On focus. On the thing it pulls out of a person when they are hooked to it. You used a girl. A beggar girl from the docks. You used her until there was nothing left. I found her body. In the canal behind your warehouse.”

For the first time, a ripple went through the room. De Wit shifted in his chair. Scholten’s lips pursed. A dead magnate from a centuries-old wreck was one thing. A dead beggar girl in a modern canal was another. It was messy. It was local.

Visser set his glass down with a soft, definitive click. “This is slander of the most grotesque kind. You have produced a fictional journal and the corpse of an unfortunate urchin. You are clearly unwell. Grief-stricken over your colleague, perhaps. Or simply ambitious. You seek to damage me. For what? More money?”

He was so calm. So reasonable. He turned to the others. “Gentlemen, I brought you here to dispel this nonsense. The stone is a curiosity. A piezoelectric oddity I am studying for potential applications in timekeeping. The girl’s death is a tragedy, but it has nothing to do with me. This woman is a disgruntled former employee, collaborating with elements in the city who wish to see my new manufactory fail.” His gaze slid to Rafael’s back by the window. “She is a pawn. And this is a waste of our time.”

The balance was tipping. I could see it in their faces. Visser’s narrative was clean, efficient. Mine was a gothic horror story, dripping with mud and madness. They were men of ledgers and tonnage. The supernatural was bad business.

I stood up. The movement was abrupt. It broke the rhythm of the room.

“It’s not a story,” I said, my voice low but sharp. “It’s a machine. And he’s building it. Bigger. He doesn’t want to power a single loom. He wants to power a factory. A city. And he’ll need more than beggar girls for that. He’ll need a source. A strong source.”

I looked at each of them. “You think you’re safe. You think your money, your walls, your names protect you. But you’re just a different kind of fuel to him. You’re a variable. And when he balances his equation, you’ll be factored in. Or out.”

Scholten spoke, his voice a dry rustle. “You are speaking in hysterics, woman.”

“Am I?” I took a step toward Visser. He didn’t flinch. “Where is the stone now, Mijnheer Visser? In your workshop? In your safe? Or is it already in the new foundation, the one you’re pouring for the mill on the Amstel? The one that will never need a coal bill? The one that will out-produce every other concern in the city? The one that will make you not just rich, but indispensable?”

That landed. I saw it hit. The mill on the Amstel was Visser’s open secret, his next great leap. A project on a scale that required their collective investment, their silent approval. I had aimed the wrench, and I had struck metal.

Visser’s polish finally cracked. A thin, vertical line appeared between his brows. “You have been poking around where you do not belong.”

“Someone has to,” I said. “Since the police see only what you pay them to see.”

Rafael turned from the window. He did not look at me. He addressed the room. “The accusation is specific. The journal exists. The girl’s body exists. The connection to Pieter’s death is circumstantial, but persistent.” He paused, letting the words settle. “And there is the matter of the stone itself. I have seen it. Its properties are… unnatural. Mijnheer Visser’s research into its application has been pursued with unusual secrecy. For a mere timekeeping device.”

He was not siding with me. He was stating facts. But in this room, facts were weapons.

Visser stood. He was taller than I remembered. The firelight cast his shadow, long and grasping, across the bookshelves. “This is absurd. You would take the word of this… this dredged-up creature… over mine? Based on a madman’s diary and the fantasies of a jealous rival?” His eyes burned at Rafael.

“I would see the stone,” Van Dijk said quietly. “This Luz thing. I would see this machine. The small one. The loom.”

“As would I,” said De Wit, crossing his arms over his broad chest.

It was a check. Not a checkmate. They were not convinced of my truth, but they were now uncertain of his. The stink of failure, of mess, was in the air.

Visser’s jaw tightened. He smoothed his waistcoat, a quick, violent gesture. “It is in a secure location. For study.”

“Then we will go to it,” Scholten said. “Now. We will all go. We will see this wonder, or this fraud, for ourselves.”

Panic, cold and bright, flashed in Visser’s eyes for a single, unguarded moment. It was the first real emotion I had seen from him. It was more telling than any confession. The stone was not somewhere he could show them. Not in its current state. Not with what it was doing.

“That is not possible today,” he said, his voice strained.

“Why not?” I pressed. “Is it busy? Is it… feeding?”

The word hung in the cigar-scented air. Ugly. Primitive.

Visser looked from one man to the next. He saw the doubt hardening into decision. His authority, that invisible currency, was bleeding away. He turned his fury on me, pure and undiluted.

“You,” he hissed. “You think you have won something? You have won nothing. You are a dead woman walking. You always have been. From the moment you pulled that stone from the mud, you were a spent match. A used rag.”

He took a step toward me. The men in their chairs did not move. Rafael watched, impassive.

“You want to see the machine?” Visser’s voice dropped to a venomous whisper. “You want to understand what it can do? Fine. Come to the Amstel site. Tonight. Midnight. Come and see the future. Come and see what happens to variables that have outlived their usefulness.”

It was an invitation to an execution. Everyone in the room knew it.

But it was also a capitulation. He was abandoning the field of polite lies. He was moving the battle to ground of his choosing, where the rules were written in violence.

He straightened, regaining his composure like a man adjusting a cuff. He nodded to the others, a stiff, formal gesture. “Gentlemen. I apologize for this spectacle. The matter will be settled. Cleanly. You have my word.”

He did not look at me again. He walked to the door, opened it, and was gone. His footsteps faded down the hall.

The silence he left behind was thick and charged. De Wit cleared his throat. Van Dijk stared into the fire. Scholten began to make a note in a small book, his pen scratching loudly.

Rafael moved from the window. “Gentlemen. I believe the audience is concluded.”

They rose, one by one, gathering their coats and hats. They filed out without a word to me. I had been the catalyst for a reaction, and now I was the residue left behind. The door closed, leaving me alone with Rafael.

He finally looked at me. His face was tired. “You got what you wanted. Their doubt. His rage.”

“It’s a trap,” I said.

“Of course it is.”

“You won’t interfere.”

“I cannot. Not directly. He has challenged you. You have accepted. The city’s… equilibrium… now depends on the outcome.” He picked up Visser’s abandoned brandy glass, looked at it, set it down. “He will have the stone there. And the machine. It will be fully realized. He will use it to demonstrate his power. And to eliminate you.”

“So I shouldn’t go.”

He gave me a thin, humorless smile. “If you do not go, you are admitting your accusations are lies. He will be vindicated. The investors will return to him. And he will have you killed anyway, at his leisure. You have forced the crisis, Els. Now you must see it through.”

I knew he was right. The tightrope was gone. There was only the pit.

“What is the machine?” I asked. “What has he built?”

Rafael’s gaze grew distant. “I have only heard whispers. From men who drank too much and woke up afraid of what they’d said. It is not a loom. Not anymore. It is a heart. He is building a mechanical heart for his new mill, and the stone is its chamber. It does not just draw power from a single life. It resonates. It pulls from… proximity. From attention. From the collective will of those around it. A factory full of workers, focused on their tasks, their worries, their small lives… it would feed on that. A slow, perpetual harvest. He calls it the Eendracht. The Concord. Harmony.” He looked at me. “A harmony where every soul is a note, and he is the only one who hears the music.”

The horror of it was so vast it felt cold. It wasn’t murder. It was farming.

“And tonight?”

“Tonight, he will have a test subject. Someone with a strong will. A defiant spirit. To prime it. To ignite the process.” He didn’t need to say who that subject would be. “If it works… the proof of concept will be undeniable. The men who were here today will fund him. They will not care about the source of the power. They will only care that it is theirs.”

I walked to the fireplace, holding my hands out to the flames. They were steady. The hollow stillness inside me had filled with a clear, cold substance. Like the jenever Jan served. Potent. Inflammable.

“I have to break it,” I said.

“You have to try.”

I turned to face him. “You ‘find you do not care for the equation.’ But you won’t lift a finger to stop it.”

“I am lifting you,” he said simply. “It is all I can do. The city is a machine, too, Els. More complex than his. I work within its gears. To reach in and grab his would tear the whole mechanism apart. There would be chaos. Fires in the street. The kind of mess that gets everyone killed.” He stepped closer, his voice dropping. “But a single, determined variable, introduced at the precise point of failure… that can jam a single machine. Without collapsing the whole.”

It was a cold calculus. My life for a chance to stop his. A fair trade, in the economy of power.

I nodded. I understood the game now. I had never left the water. I was just in deeper, the pressure crushing, the light gone. All that was left was to swim toward the only thing I could feel: the cold, blue pulse of the stone, and the darker shadow of the man who wielded it.

“I’ll need a weapon,” I said.

Rafael reached into his coat and placed a small, heavy object on the table beside the brandy glass. It was a diver’s knife. A good one. The blade was short, thick, and cruelly sharp. The handle was worn smooth from use.

“It was Pieter’s,” he said. “He was a sentimental man. He kept it after he left the docks. For luck.”

I picked it up. The handle fit my palm as if it had been waiting there. It had no luck in it. Only purpose.

“Midnight,” I said.

“The Amstel site. The main foundation pit.” He moved to the door. “Good luck, Els. The city will not thank you. But I will remember the equation.”

Then he was gone, and I was alone with the dying fire, the ghost of cigar smoke, and the weight of the knife in my hand. The journal was still on the chair. I left it there. Its words had done their work. Now it was down to the language of blood, and steel, and a stone that drank the light from the world.

I looked out the window. The day was bright and cold. The city gleamed, a beautiful, heartless machine. I had until midnight to walk through its gears one last time, to the place where its new, hungry heart was waiting to beat.

Chapter 23

Chapter 23

The day was a lie. The cold, bright sun that glinted off the canal belts and made the gabled facades look like polished bone was a stage-light. It illuminated a city playing itself, a beautiful, intricate deception. I walked through it, a ghost in the machinery. The weight of Pieter’s knife was a secret gravity in the deep pocket of my coat, pulling me down through the cheerful bustle of the Leidsestraat, past the flower stalls with their defiant winter blooms, into the narrower, quieter streets that led toward the river.

I was a variable. A single, determined variable. Rafael’s cold calculus echoed in the rhythm of my steps. I understood the terms. My life was the coefficient. The outcome was the jam in Van der Meer’s machine. A fair trade. But fairness was a ledger for men who balanced books, not for women who balanced on the knife-edge between the gutter and the grave. I had walked that edge so long the drop was a familiar companion. Tonight, I would either fall or I would learn to fly on the thermals of the explosion.

My feet carried me without conscious direction. Not toward the Amstel site, not yet. The hours until midnight were a vast, empty plain I had to cross. I found myself on the Keizersgracht, stopping before a tall, narrow house with green shutters. My sister’s house. The curtains were drawn in the front parlor. A pram stood by the door, empty. A life contained within clean walls, defined by the cries of a child and the expectations of a husband. A life that had once been mine to grasp, if I had been willing to soften my edges, to smile at the right men, to trade my suspicion for security.

I had chosen the damp wool and the smell of fryer grease. I had chosen the calculating glances in De Visser, the currency of subtle teeth. I had chosen the deep, dark water. Looking at that pram, I felt no regret, only a colder, clearer certainty. Her world, with its rules written in invitations and gossip, was a machine, too. Just a quieter one. Its gears were made of suggestion and exclusion, and it crushed souls just as efficiently, it just left less visible blood. At least in my world, the threats were honest. A knife was a knife. A man’s greed was a naked, ugly thing. You could see it coming.

A shadow moved behind the glass of the door. I turned and walked away before it could resolve into my sister’s face, before I could see the pity or the fear or the quiet disapproval that was her own kind of weapon. I had no place there. I was a toxin in that clean system.

The afternoon bled away in a series of aimless circuits. I bought a bitter coffee from a stall and drank it standing against a wall, watching the city move. The men in their dark coats and tall hats, talking of shipments and shares. The women with their baskets and their guarded eyes. The whole dance was a complex pressure system, and I was a sudden, low pocket, a silence in the hum. I saw a young maid, barely more than a girl, being berated by a shopkeeper for a chipped jug. Her face was a mask of terrified submission, but her hands, clenched white at her sides, told a different story. A story of a variable waiting for its moment. I gave her a single, slow nod as I passed. Her eyes flicked to mine, wide with surprise, then dropped again. But her shoulders straightened, just a fraction. A seed. Planted in barren soil, perhaps. But planted.

As dusk began to stain the sky the color of a fresh bruise, I knew where I had to go. Not to prepare, but to remember. To anchor myself in the deep, dark water from which I’d come.

The docks were the city’s exposed sinew. Here, the machine wasn’t hidden behind lace curtains and polite conversation. It roared. It stank of tar and rotting fish and the sour sweat of labor. The cranes were frozen black skeletons against the purple sky. The water in the basins was thick, sluggish, oil-slicked. This was the engine room. And I had been a cog here once. A small, silent one, but a part of the grind.

I walked to the end of the Westelijke Eilanden pier, where the wind came sharp and unfettered off the IJ. The cold needled through my coat, the same precise, hateful cold as the rain on the night this began. I stood where my father had often stood, looking out at the endless, flat grey where the water met the cloud. He’d come here to think, he said. To feel the size of the world beyond the canals.

I took the knife from my pocket. Pieter’s knife. I pulled the blade from its leather sheath. The metal was dull in the twilight, but the edge, when I tested it with my thumb, was a line of pure, potential violence. A sentimental man, Rafael had said. He kept it for luck.

But sentiment was a luxury for the safe. For men who could afford to keep trophies of a past life. For my father, for Pieter, a knife was a tool. It cut rope, pried open crates, cleaned fish. It was an extension of the hand, for work. For a woman alone, walking into a foundation pit to face a man with a stone that drank light, a knife was not a tool. It was a last, desperate argument. It was the final, sharp punctuation to a life of questions.

A voice, rough with phlegm and cheap gin, slithered out of the shadows of a stacked pallet. “That’s a pretty piece. A bit big for the likes of you, isn’t it, meisje?”

I didn’t turn. I kept looking at the water, my fingers tightening on the handle. I knew the type. The docks had its own ecosystem of predators, the ones who couldn’t even hack it as stevedores, who fed on the weaker strays. I was a woman alone. I was a provocation.

“I said,” the voice came closer, boots scraping on the wet stone. “That’s a pretty piece. Why don’t you let me have a look?”

I could smell him now. Gin, unwashed wool, the sweet-rot smell of a bad tooth. The calculus was immediate. A fight here, now, was noise. It was attention. It was a variable introduced too early, in the wrong equation. It could spoil everything.

He was close enough that I could hear his breathing. “Cat got your tongue? Or are you just stupid?”

This was the other language. The one they understood. I had shown my teeth in De Visser and made them look away. This one required a sharper demonstration.

In one motion, I pivoted. Not away, but into him. I let the momentum carry my arm, the knife a dark blur. I didn’t aim for his body. I aimed for the space he occupied, for his threat. The blade stopped a hair’s breadth from the hollow of his throat. I had stepped inside his guard, my body close to his, the knife hand rock-steady. My other hand was a fist in the greasy wool of his coat.

His eyes were wide, the whites gleaming in the gloom. The gin-sour breath hitched in his chest.

“No,” I said, my voice lower than the wind. “It is not too big for me. And you are in my light.”

I held the pose, letting him feel the certainty in my grip, the absolute lack of tremor. Letting him understand that this was not a bluff, that I was not a meisje, not a stray cat. I was a woman with a diver’s knife and nothing left to lose. The gender of the thing meant nothing. The power did. And for this frozen moment, the power was a cold line against his throat.

He swallowed. A thick, wet sound. I saw the decision flicker in his eyes: the bruised pride, the rising anger, and beneath it, the animal fear of the sharp, hard thing pressing into his skin. Fear won. It usually did.

I pushed him back, just enough to break contact. I didn’t step away. I made him step back. One shuffling step, then another. He turned and scuttled into the deeper shadows, a rat returning to its hole.

I sheathed the knife and put it back in my pocket. My heart was a slow, heavy drum. No adrenaline, no fear. Just a cold confirmation. The language worked. It was the only one that ever did.

I looked back at the water. The last of the light was gone. The city behind me was a constellation of yellow windows, a map of a thousand contained lives. Ahead was only the dark and the deep. My father had taught me that in the water, panic was death. You had to accept the pressure, the cold, the lack of light. You had to become a part of it. Then you could move.

Van der Meer’s world was the same. A different kind of pressure, a more subtle cold, a more deceptive darkness. But the principle held. I had to stop fighting the fact of it. I had to accept that I was in it, that it would crush me if it could. And then I had to move.

I left the pier and began the long walk to the Amstel. The streets grew quieter, the respectable glow of the canal belts giving way to the scattered, industrial glow of the developing south. The site was a scar on the city’s edge. A vast, ordered chaos of mud, timber, and ambition. During the day, it swarmed with men and machines. Now, under a moonless sky, it was a tomb for giants. The silhouettes of cranes stood like sentinels. Piles of brick and timber were dark mounds. The main foundation pit, the heart of Van der Meer’s new palace of commerce, was a gash of deeper blackness in the earth.

A high wooden fence surrounded it, but a section had been loosened, a single plank pried back just enough for a slender variable to slip through. Rafael’s work. I paused, listening. The wind moaned through the girders. Somewhere, a loose sheet of tin clattered. No voices. No guards. Van der Meer would not post guards. He would not share this moment. This was his ritual, his private communion with power. He would be alone. He believed himself secure within the gears of his own machine.

I slipped through the gap.

The ground was churned mud, frozen into treacherous ruts. I moved from shadow to shadow, from the hulk of a silent steam shovel to the lee of a mortar mixer. The pit lay ahead. I approached its edge and looked down.

It was a square hole, thirty feet to the bottom. The earth walls were shored up with timber. At the center, the foundation stone had been laid weeks ago—a massive block of granite. But before it, a smaller, simpler stone had been placed. A pedestal. And on that pedestal, resting on a cushion of black velvet even in the mud, was the Zeevonk.

It pulsed.

It was not a light, but an anti-light. A slow, rhythmic drawing-in of the darkness around it. A blue so deep it was almost black, a heartbeat of void. It made the night around it seem faint, insubstantial. Looking at it was like looking at a hole in the world, a hole that led to a cold, hungry depth where no light had ever been. The stone from the deep. The heart of the machine.

And standing before it, his back to me, was Van der Meer.

He wore a formal black coat, as if for a board meeting. His hands were clasped behind his back. He was utterly still, a man in prayer. The eerie, draining pulse of the stone washed over him, making him a silhouette cut from the fabric of the night itself.

This was it. The point of failure. The place where the variable had to be introduced.

There was a ladder fixed to the side of the pit, leading down. I put my hand on the cold wood. Then I stopped.

A whisper of sound. Not from the pit. From behind me.

I melted back against the timber shoring, pulling my coat close. Two figures emerged from between piles of brick. Not workmen. Their movements were too fluid, too quiet. They were dressed in dark, close-fitting clothes. They carried no lanterns. They moved like they knew the dark.

They went to the edge of the pit, not far from where I stood, and looked down at their master and his prize. They did not speak. They simply took up positions, one on either side of the ladder, facing outwards. Guards. Not the uniformed kind. The other kind. The kind Rafael had warned me about. The kind that worked in the shadows, that cleaned up messes. Van der Meer was not a fool. He was a man who calculated risk. He would not come alone. He had brought his own gears, his own teeth.

The calculus changed. Drastically.

One man, I might have surprised. One man, with the cold ferocity of nothing left to lose, I might have reached. Two professional killers, watching the only entrance, changed the equation from difficult to impossible. I was not a soldier. I was a woman with a knife and a death wish. They were the embodiment of the machine’s immune system. They would swat me aside before I was halfway down the ladder.

The seed of true resolution, planted in the darkness of this all-is-lost moment, began to stir. It was not a plan. It was a memory. The maid’s clenched hands. The dock rat’s fearful retreat. The language of power was not always direct force. Sometimes it was leverage. Sometimes it was understanding the pressure points of a different machine.

Van der Meer’s machine was powered by greed, by control. But it was populated by people. And people had other pressures. Other loyalties. Other fears.

I looked at the two guards. They were part of his system, but they were not him. They were tools. And tools could be turned, if you found the right weakness, if you applied pressure to the right point.

I closed my eyes, shutting out the pulse of the stone, and thought. Rafael’s words. The city is a machine, too. More complex than his. I had been trying to jam Van der Meer’s machine directly. A frontal assault. But Rafael worked within the city’s gears. He used its complexity.

I could not fight these men. But the city could.

I slipped away from the pit’s edge, back into the maze of construction. I moved quickly now, with purpose. I emerged from the site at a different point, circling around to the nearest proper street. It was deserted, lined with darkened workshops. But a few blocks away, I knew there was a politiebureau. A small, sleepy outpost for a district that was mostly mud and ambition.

I did not run toward it. I walked. But my walk was the walk of the docks, of De Visser. The walk of a woman who was not a victim. I pushed open the door to the station house.

The air inside was warm and stale, smelling of coal smoke, cheap paper, and old sweat. A single, weary officer in a rumpled uniform sat behind a high desk, writing in a ledger by the light of an oil lamp. He looked up, his expression shifting from boredom to mild annoyance at the interruption.

“Ja? What is it?”

I did not approach as a supplicant. I stood just inside the door, letting the cold air from outside swirl around me. I met his eyes and held them. I spoke in the flat, uninflected tone of someone reporting a fact, not asking for help.

“There is a theft in progress at the Van der Meer construction site on the Amstel. The new foundation. Men with tools. They are breaking into the site office.”

The officer’s eyebrows lifted. “Van der Meer’s site? And who are you?”

“A night-watchman’s daughter. He is sick. I walk his round.” The lie came easily, dressed in the plain cloth of truth. “I saw them. Three men. They had crowbars.”

I leaned forward slightly, just enough to let the lamplight catch the earnest fear I forced into my eyes. The other language. “The heer Van der Meer… he is a strict man. If there is theft and my father is not there… it will be his position. Please. If you come quickly, perhaps you can catch them.”

I invoked the two pressures the city’s machine understood: property and patronage. The threat to a rich man’s property, and the potential downfall of a poor man to a rich man’s wrath. It was a story this officer could process. A woman reporting a crime was suspect. A daughter trying to save her father’s job was sympathetic. A chance to be seen doing his duty for a powerful man was opportunity.

He hesitated for only a second, weighing the chill of the night against the potential benefits. Duty, and a chance to curry favor, won. He heaved himself to his feet, grabbing his hat and a heavy wooden baton from a hook.

“Show me.”

“They were at the east fence, by the office shed,” I said, pointing back the way I came. “I… I dare not go closer. My father would kill me.”

He grunted, a sound of masculine understanding. “Wait here.”

He strode out into the night, his boots ringing on the cobbles. I did not wait. The moment the door swung shut, I was moving again, a shadow retracing my steps, but on a different vector.

By the time the officer’s shouted “Halt! Politie!” echoed across the silent site, I was back at the edge of the foundation pit. The scene below had changed.

Van der Meer had turned from the stone. He was looking up, his face a pale mask of fury in the negative light. His two guards were no longer at the ladder. They were gone, lured away by the disturbance at the far end of the site—the sound of their master’s property being threatened, the blundering intrusion of the city’s own clumsy gear.

It was the only window I would get.

I did not use the ladder. I sat on the edge of the pit, turned, and dropped, my feet finding the rough timber shoring, my hands scraping down the cold earth as I half-slid, half-fell the thirty feet to the bottom. I landed in a crouch, the impact jarring my knees, mud soaking instantly through my clothes.

Van der Meer stared. For a second, pure, uncomprehending astonishment wiped the fury from his face. He saw a mud-smeared woman rising from the ground of his sacred pit. I was an impossibility in his calculus.

“You,” he breathed. The word was not fear. It was disgust. The disgust of a master clockmaker finding a slug in his gear train.

I pulled Pieter’s knife from my pocket. The leather sheath fell to the mud. The blade, freed, seemed to drink the faint, stolen light from the stone.

“The variable,” I said.

His astonishment hardened into contempt. He didn’t call for his guards. He didn’t move. He simply stood between me and the pulsing Zeevonk, as if his presence alone were a barrier I could not cross.

“Els de Vries,” he said, his voice regaining its smooth, oiled control. “The diver’s daughter. The little rat from the docks. Rafael’s errand girl. I should have had you smothered in your cradle.” He took a step forward, not away from me, but toward me. “Do you think that toy makes you a threat? You are a flaw. A speck of grit. And I am the millstone.”

He was not a physical man. He was soft where my father had been hard, pale where Pieter had been weathered. But his power was not in his body. It was in his certainty. He believed, utterly, in the hierarchy of the world. He was at the top. I was at the bottom. The knife in my hand was a violation of natural law.

“You took my father,” I said, beginning to circle, forcing him to turn, to keep the stone in my sight. “You took Pieter. You are taking the soul of this city, piece by piece, to feed that thing.”

“I am giving this city a soul!” he snapped, the control cracking for an instant, revealing the fanatic beneath. “A new heart! One that beats with order, with power, with purpose! Not the weak, sentimental flutter of the past. Your father was a relic. Pieter was a fool who thought he could bargain with the deep. They were consumed by the old world. I am building the new one. And you…” He smiled, a thin, cruel stretch of the lips. “You are just a sacrifice it requires. The final piece. The unexpected variable that perfects the equation. The deep demands a price, girl. It always has. Why do you think the sea takes men? It is a transaction.”

He lunged then. Not at me, but to the side, toward the pedestal. His hand closed around the Zeevonk.

The effect was instantaneous.

The slow, sucking pulse became a violent surge. The blue-black void-light erupted, not outwards, but inwards, into him. It raced up his arm, veins of anti-light branching under his skin. His back arched. A sound tore from his throat that was not human—a grating, resonant shriek, like stone grinding on stone. The air turned brittle and cold. The mud at our feet began to frost over.

He was not controlling the stone. He was merging with it. The machine and its maker becoming one.

His eyes opened. They were no longer his eyes. They were pools of the same hungry void, the same deep blue-black. He turned them on me, and I felt it—a pulling, a draining. Not of light, but of will. Of heat. Of life. It was the feeling of the deep pressure, magnified a thousand times, focused into a gaze.

“See,” the thing that was Van der Meer said, its voice a chorus of whispers from a cold abyss. “See the heart of the new world.”

My limbs felt heavy. The knife in my hand seemed like a absurd twig. The cold was inside me, in my bones, in my blood. This was the true machine. Not gears of iron, but gears of stolen essence, of consumed lives. And I was next in line.

But the seed, planted in the darkest moment, broke through.

A single, determined variable, introduced at the precise point of failure…

The point of failure was not Van der Meer. It was the transaction. The price. The deep demanded a sacrifice. He had offered my father, Pieter, the city itself. But the deep, like the city, was a machine. And it could be jammed.

I did not fight the cold. I did not try to strike at him. I accepted the pressure, the crushing, soul-sucking void. I stopped being a woman trying to kill a man. I became the variable.

I threw the knife.

Not at Van der Meer. At the stone.

Pieter’s knife. The diver’s tool. The sentimental man’s luck. It was not steel aimed at a heart. It was a symbol aimed at a transaction.

The blade tumbled, a flash of dull metal in the negation of light. It struck the Zeevonk not with a clang, but with a sound like a bell cracking under ice.

And it stuck.

The pulsing stopped. The inward surge of void-light froze. The chorus of whispers became a single, sharp, silent scream of wrongness.

Van der Meer stared at the knife hilt protruding from the heart of his power. The void-light in his eyes flickered, confused. The transaction was corrupted. The sacrifice was tainted. A tool, a memory, a piece of the sentimental, human world he despised, was lodged in the heart of his perfect machine.

“No…” he whispered, his own voice returning, ragged with pain and disbelief. “No, it… it is not… the equation…”

He staggered, clutching his chest as if the knife were in him. The connection was breaking. The stone began to vibrate, a high, painful whine that shivered in the teeth. Cracks, lines of real, ordinary black, spiderwebbed across its blue-black surface.

The machine was jamming.

He turned his desperate, human eyes to me. The hatred in them was pure, undiluted, the last fuel in a dying engine. “You ruin… everything…”

From above, shouts. The clatter of boots on the ladder. His guards, returning. The police officer, confused and shouting. The city’s gears, intruding.

Van der Meer made a last, desperate grab for the stone, for the knife, to pull it free, to reset the equation.

I didn’t stop him. I just watched as his fingers closed around the hilt of Pieter’s knife.

The Zeevonk shattered.

It did not explode. It imploded. There was a sound like a vast intake of breath, and then a silent, concussive wave of force that had no light, no heat, only a profound, nullifying absence. It blew outwards from the pedestal.

It hit Van der Meer first. He simply… unraveled. His form smeared, like ink in water, and was sucked into the collapsing point where the stone had been. There was no scream. Just a final, vanishing sigh.

The wave hit me.

It was not pain. It was the total negation of feeling. Of thought. Of self. For an eternity that lasted less than a second, I did not exist. I was the variable consumed by the failed equation.

Then the world rushed back in. Sound: shouts, screams, the groan of timber. Sensation: the frozen mud against my cheek, the taste of dirt and iron in my mouth. I was lying on my side, ten feet from where I had stood. The pedestal was empty. Of the stone, of the knife, of Van der Meer, there was no sign. Only a shallow, perfectly smooth depression in the earth, as if something had been neatly, surgically, erased.

Boots splashed through mud. Rough hands grabbed my shoulders, rolled me over. I looked up into the face of one of Van der Meer’s guards. His professional calm was gone. His face was a rictus of terror and fury. Behind him, the other guard held the bewildered police officer at bay, a knife to his throat.

The guard who held me shook me, his fingers digging into my collarbone. “What did you do? What did you do?”

I looked past him, up at the rim of the pit. Other figures were gathering, silhouetted against the night sky—workmen roused by the noise, more police. The city’s machine was waking up to the chaos in one of its newest gears.

I looked back at the guard. I smiled. It was a show of teeth, bloody and cold.

“I jammed the machine,” I whispered.

His fist drew back. I saw it coming. I didn’t try to move. The darkness after the wave was still in me, a comforting blanket. Let it end here. Let the final, crushing gear do its work.

But the blow never landed.

A new voice, sharp with authority, cut through the din. “Release her. Now.”

The guard froze. He looked up.

Standing at the top of the pit, flanked by four men in dark coats who were not police, was Rafael. He held no weapon. He needed none. His presence was a different kind of pressure. He looked down, his face impassive, taking in the scene: the empty depression, the missing stone, the missing magnate, the mud-smeared woman in the grip of a thug.

“This is a city matter now,” Rafael said, his voice carrying the weight of the hidden gears. “Release her to me.”

The guard’s eyes darted from Rafael to his partner, to the growing crowd. The calculus changed again. Their master was gone. Their purpose was void. The city’s official machine, and its hidden counterpart, were converging. To fight was to be ground to paste between them.

He let me go, shoving me back into the mud. He and his partner melted into the shadows of the pit, disappearing into the construction maze like the rats they were. The police officer stumbled free, clutching his throat, babbling about thieves and ghosts.

Rafael’s men descended the ladder. They did not help me up. They formed a cordon around me. Rafael climbed down last, picking his way fastidiously through the mud. He stopped before the smooth, empty depression. He looked at it for a long moment. Then he looked at me.

“The stone?” he asked, quietly.

“Gone.”

“Van der Meer?”

“Gone with it.”

He nodded slowly. The equation had been solved. Not as he’d planned. The variable had not been eliminated; it had corrupted the entire formula. “And the knife?”

“Gone too.”

A faint, almost imperceptible smile touched his lips. “Pieter would have appreciated the irony.” He stepped closer, his voice dropping so only I could hear. “You understand, this changes nothing. The machine—the city’s machine—must still turn. There will be an inquiry. A story. A wealthy man disappears at his construction site. A strange woman found at the scene. The gears will demand a narrative.”

I pushed myself to my knees. The cold was settling into my bones, the deep, permanent cold of the void. “What narrative?”

He looked at me, and in his eyes I saw the final, cold truth of his world. “That depends on you, Els. You can be the tragic victim. The madwoman. The thief. Or…” He paused. “You can be a variable that is absorbed. A useful piece in a new configuration. The woman who witnessed a tragedy, who helped bring clarity. The choice is yours. But you must choose now. Before the larger gears engage.”

He was offering me a place within the machine. Not as a victim, not as grit to be ground away, but as a cog. A small, silent one, perhaps. But a part of the grind. It was the only survival his world permitted.

Above us, the official voices grew louder. Lanterns bobbed. The city was here, in all its clumsy, hungry majesty.

I looked at the empty space where the heart of a new world had almost beaten. I looked at my muddy, empty hands. I had swum toward the cold, blue pulse, and I had found only a deeper dark. I had jammed the machine. But the city’s machine, the old, complex, heartless one, was still here. It would always be here.

I met Rafael’s gaze. I had no teeth left to show. Only a bottomless cold, and a single, final question.

“What,” I asked, my voice raw with the chill of the deep, “do you need me to say?”

Chapter 24

Chapter 24

The rain was a soft, persistent murmur on the cobbles, a gentle afterthought to the storm that had broken the world. It soaked into the shoulders of my new coat—thick, grey wool, government-issue—with a patient indifference. It did not needle. It simply was. I walked, my footsteps a measured, official sound against the wet stone, the echo contained by the high, gabled facades of the Herengracht.

The warmth inside the Binnenhof committee room was a controlled, dry heat, smelling of polished wood, ink, and the faint, waxy scent of power. The low murmur of men in dark suits hit a lull as I entered. Not many women came in here alone, not at this hour, not dressed like this: a clean, pressed cog in the state’s machine. I met the glances of the committee members, held them for the exact, prescribed beat—respectful, but not challenging—until they returned to their papers. A show of compliance was the only currency they understood in here.

“Mevrouw Visser.” The chairman, a man with a face like a folded ledger, nodded from the high bench. He did not smile. He gestured to the solitary chair placed in the pool of light from the green-shaded lamp. Testimony expected now, in precise words and in a specific order. Always order, in this case.

I took the seat, my fingers resting on the cool, varnished wood of the table before me. They were clean. The nails were short, unbroken. There was no grit under them.

“For the final time, for the official record,” the chairman intoned, his voice dust-dry. “Describe the events of the night of the twenty-third, at the Van der Meer construction site.”

I drew a breath. The air here had no fryer grease, no brine. It had no taste at all.

“I was conducting a follow-up inquiry,” I began, the words smooth and familiar from repetition, “into the disappearance of my client, Meneer Pieter van der Linden, as previously detailed in my earlier statements. My contract, as entered into evidence, permitted such activity. I had reason to believe Meneer van der Meer might have information.”

The story unspooled from me, a clean, efficient thread. A concerned brother. A corrupt developer. A deal gone wrong. A tragic confrontation. I was a witness. A professional doing a job, caught in a dangerous situation between two ruthless men. I had seen van der Meer, agitated, holding a strange artifact—a geological oddity, perhaps, from his dredging work. In the struggle with van der Meer’s man, Rafael—a known fixer with a history of violence—the artifact was lost to the waters. Van der Meer, in his panic, had fallen. A terrible accident. A tragedy.

I had tried to help. I had called the authorities.

The words were a kind of masonry, building a plausible, sturdy wall behind which the truth could lie buried, a cold, blue pulse extinguished in the deep silt. I was the bricklayer. My payment was my continued existence within the walled city.

“And your relationship with the individual known as Rafael?” another committee member asked, his eyes sharp behind his spectacles.

“Professional. Antagonistic. He was an agent of Meneer van der Meer. He attempted to impede my investigation. He is currently assisting with inquiries elsewhere.” My voice was flat. Neutral. Rafael was in another room, in another building, likely giving a different but complementary version of this calibrated truth. We were gears now, meshing silently to turn the larger mechanism. He had his place. I had mine. The machine demanded synergy, not conflict.

The chairman made a note. “And the artifact? This… ‘stone’?”

“As I said. Lost. It seemed to be a point of obsession for Meneer van der Meer. Of no material value, I should think. Just a rock.”

They nodded. A rock was a good thing to lose. It asked no questions, demanded no audits.

The inquiry lasted another hour. They picked at the edges of my narrative, not to tear it, but to seam it more neatly into the official tapestry. The story was needed. The city’s gears—the press, the courts, the civic record—demanded a narrative. The wealthy, overreaching developer, killed by his own ambition. The brave, if unfortunate, brother lost earlier to the man’s ruthlessness. A tidy circle. A closed case.

I was the useful variable that had been absorbed. The woman who had witnessed, who had helped bring clarity. Not a victim. Not a madwoman. A functionary of resolution.

When they dismissed me, it was with a curt thanks. My coat was waiting on the stand. I put it on, feeling the weight of the good wool, the broad cut that hid the shape beneath. I walked out into the central courtyard of the Binnenhof. The rain had softened to a mist, blurring the sharp Gothic lines of the Hall of Knights into something mournful and distant.

A figure detached itself from the shadow of a pillar. Rafael. He fell into step beside me, our footsteps matching on the wet flagstones. He wore a dark, well-tailored suit. He looked like a senior clerk. A trusted one.

“It is done,” he said, not looking at me.

“It is done.”

“Your statement was satisfactory.”

“As was yours, I assume.”

A faint, almost imperceptible nod. The irony was not lost on either of us. We had tried to destroy each other. Now our survival was mutually assured by the lie we told in unison. The ultimate transaction.

“They have offered you a position,” he said as we passed under the archway, out toward the street. “The new municipal liaison office. Investigating certain… delicate matters. Matters where a woman’s perspective might be useful for public perception.”

I had known the offer was coming. It had been implied in the chairman’s final, assessing look. The machine always found a use for a tool that had proven durable.

“And you?” I asked.

“A similar role. Different department. We will not need to see each other often.”

That was a relief, written in the cold air between us. We stopped at the canal’s edge. The water was grey, opaque, holding its secrets close.

“He would have hated this,” Rafael said quietly. “Van der Meer. All that power, that vision… reduced to a clerical error. A footnote in a committee report.”

“He wanted a new world,” I said. “The old one just digested him.”

Rafael turned to look at me then, and I saw the man who had pointed a knife at my heart, who had bargained with monsters. But the fervor was gone. The knife was gone. In his eyes was only the flat, efficient sheen of a man who balances ledgers that deal in lives. “There is no new world, Els. There is only the machine. You can be outside it, and be crushed. Or you can be inside it, and turn. Those are the only coordinates.”

He touched the brim of his hat—a polite, meaningless gesture—and walked away, his form dissolving into the mist and the bureaucrats streaming out for their midday meal.

I stood alone. I had my life. I had a job that would pay in coin, not in kind. I had a story that would hold. I had been pulled from the void and slotted neatly into the great, grinding clockwork of the city. I was safe. I was warm. I was clean.

The cold I felt then had nothing to do with the weather. It was the cold of the deep, the abyssal chill I had chosen to swim away from. I had jammed the machine of a mad god, only to become a lubricated part of the older, subtler, more patient one. I had shown my teeth so many times, only to learn that in this place, the real power lay in having no teeth at all. In having a smooth, blank face that asked no questions that mattered, that told only the stories it was fed.

I began to walk, not toward the new municipal offices, but away from them, my feet carrying me without conscious direction. The grand canals gave way to narrower waterways, the fine houses to workshops and warehouses. The smell of the city changed—less wax and polish, more damp brick, rust, and the distant, familiar tang of the sea.

I found myself on a familiar bridge. The rain had stopped. Below, the water of the Oudezijds was the color of tarnished lead. I looked down at my reflection in the murky surface: a woman in a grey coat, her face pale and indistinct. A respectable ghost.

My hands, buried in the deep pockets, closed into fists. They were empty. They were always empty now. But in my mind, I felt the ghost-weight of the stone—not the god-thing, but the first one, the river-smoothed piece of granite Pieter had given me a lifetime ago. A paperweight. A reminder of a different kind of depth. I had thrown it into the sea to save a man who was already dead. A futile, human gesture.

From the pocket of the fine coat, my fingers retrieved not the stone, but a single, worn guilden. My last coin from the old life. I turned it over. The metal was warm from my skin.

I did not throw it in. I held it tight. A show of teeth was not currency here. But perhaps a single, stubborn coin of one’s own could be. Not to buy anything. Just to prove you still had something that wasn’t issued to you.

I walked off the bridge. The streets grew tighter, darker, smelling of life lived raw and close. I heard the clatter of a cart, the shout of a fishmonger closing up, the wail of a child from an open window above. The city’s true sound, beneath the murmur of committees.

And then I saw the sign, swinging gently in the damp breeze: De Visser. The paint was more faded than I remembered. One of the windows was cracked, patched with tape.

I stopped. The warmth from within would be a physical blow, thick with the smell of stale beer and brine and hard work. The conversation would be a low, guttural murmur. Heads would turn if I walked in. Not many women came in here alone, not at this hour, not looking like I did: a respectable ghost haunting the wrong neighborhood.

Jan would be behind the zinc counter. He would not smile. He would see the coat, the clean hands, the empty, official look in my eyes. He might nod. He might not. He would push a chipped tumbler of jenever across the wood. Payment expected later.

In coin, or in kind.

I stood there for a long time, on the threshold of the rain-darkened street and the yellow light pooling from the tavern window. The choice was not the one Rafael had given me. That choice was already made. The machine had me.

This was a different choice. A smaller one. A choice of rooms. Of which kind of warmth to pretend could thaw the deep cold inside.

I could walk in. I could take the glass. I could let them see the ghost, let them whisper. I could pay in the coin I had, and see what, if anything, I got in kind.

Or I could turn, and walk back toward the wide, clean canals, to the office with the dry heat and the taste of nothing, to the life that had been given to me, where I would be a useful, silent cog, turning in the dark, telling the stories I was fed until I forgot I had a voice of my own.

The mist beaded on the wool of my government-issue coat. I looked at my reflection in the tavern window—a blurred, grey shape against the glow of human noise.

Slowly, I unbuttoned the coat. I took it off. I folded it over my arm, hiding the institutional grey. Beneath, I wore a simple, dark dress. It was my own.

I pushed open the door of De Visser.

The warmth was a physical blow. The smell hit me—stale beer, brine, the tang of worked bodies—like a forgotten language. The low murmur stumbled, then died. Heads turned.
